Ruling on Passing the Miqat Without Ihram
Ruling on passing miqat without ihram and return to miqat with ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for one who passes the miqat without ihram and explain the conditions under which the dam penalty is dropped, distinguishing the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Passing the miqat without ihram obligates a dam; returning with talbiyah at the miqat drops it.

When a person intends Hajj or Umrah, they must enter ihram at the designated miqat. If they pass the miqat without ihram, a blood sacrifice (dam) becomes wajib. However, if they return to the miqat and assume ihram with talbiyah, the dam is waived. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the role of talbiyah, and related conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is passing the miqat without being in ihram. The default ruling is that a dam becomes wajib. Understand that this is a penalty for missing the obligatory act of assuming ihram at the proper boundary.
This is agreed upon by the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the condition that drops the dam
If the person returns to any miqat (not necessarily the one they passed) and becomes a muhrim with talbiyah, the dam is dropped. The text emphasizes that the return must be before starting the rituals of Hajj/Umrah (e.g., before circumambulation).
Talbiyah is crucial according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that talbiyah at the miqat is necessary for the dam to be dropped. The Sahibayn argue that simply becoming muhrim at the miqat suffices, even without talbiyah. Identify their respective proofs.
The Sahibayn analogize to one who assumes ihram from home and passes the miqat silently — no dam is owed.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Sahibayn
The author responds that assuming ihram from home is the primary determination (azimah), but delaying to the miqat is a concession (rukhsah). Therefore, the right of the miqat must be fulfilled by initiating talbiyah there.
This reasoning is specific to Imam Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a person returns to the miqat as a muhrim without talbiyah, according to Abu Hanifa the dam is still owed; according to the Sahibayn it is dropped. The mufta bihi is not specified in this passage, but later Hanafi works may give tarjih.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate; both views are based on valid usul.

Source Text

باب مجاوزة الميقات بغير إحرام من جاوز الميقات غير محرم ثم عاد محرماًملبياً أو جاوز ثم أحرم بعمرة ثم أفسد وقضى بطل الدم أي من جاوز آخر المواقيت بغير إحرام ثم عاد إليه وهو محرم ولبى فيه فقد سقط عنه الدم الذي لزمه بالمجاوزة بغير إحرام لأن قد تدارك ما فاته. أطلق الإحرام فشمل إحرام الحج فرضاً كان أو نفلاً وإحرام العمرة وأشار إلى أنه لو عاد بغير إحرام ثم أحرم منه فإنه يسقط الدم بالأولى لأنه أنشأ التلبية الواجبة عند ابتداء الإحرام، ولهذا كان السقوط متفقاً عليه . وقيد بكونه ملبياً في الميقات لأنه لو عاد محرماً ولم يلب في الميقات فإنه لا يسقط الدم عنه وهو قول الإمام لأنه لا يكون متداركاً لما فاته إلا بها، وعندهما يسقط الدم مطلقاً كما لو أحرم من دوبرة أهله ومر بالمواقيت ساكتاً فإنه لا شيء عليه اتفاقاً. وجوابه أن الإحرام من دوبرة أهله هو العزيمة وقد أتى به فإذا ترخص بالتأخير إلى الميقات وجب عليه قضاء حقه بإنشاء التلبية. وأشار إلى أنه لو عاد محرماً ولم يلب فيه لكن لبي بعدما جاوزه ثم رجع ومر به ساكتاً فإنه يسقط عنه بالأولى لأنه فوق الواجب عليه في تعظيم البيت. وأطلق في العود فشمل ما إذا عاد إلى الميقات الذي جاوزه غير محرم أو إلى غيره أقرب أو أبعد لأن المواقيت كلها سواء في حق الإحرام والأولى أن يحرم من وقته. كذا في المحيط. وقيدنا بكونه جاوز آخر المواقيت لما قدمناه في باب الإحرام أنه لا يجب إلا عند آخرها، ويجوز مجاوزة ميقاته بغير إحرام إذا كان بعده ميقات :آخر وترك المصنف قيداً لا بد منه وهو يكون العود إلى الميقات قبل الشروع في الأعمال، فلو عاد إليه بعد ما طاف شوطاً لا يسقط باب مجاوزة الميقات بغير إحرام أن قول المصنف : (من جاوز الميقات غير محرم قال في النهر كان عليه أن يقول لزمه الدم إلا أنه اكتفى بما فهم اقتضاء من قوله بطل الدم». قوله : (وما في الهداية من التقييد باستلام الحجر) أي حيث قال : لو عاد بعد ما ابتدأ الطواف
Chapter: Passing the Miqat without Ihram. Whoever passes the miqat without being in ihram and then returns as a muhrim performing talbiyah, or passes and then assumes ihram for Umrah and then invalidates and makes up, the blood sacrifice is void. That is, whoever passes the last of the miqats without ihram and then returns to it while he is a Muhrim and performs talbiyah in it, the blood sacrifice that was obligatory upon him due to passing without ihram is dropped from him because he has made up what he missed. He used the absolute term 'ihram', so it includes the ihram for Hajj whether obligatory or supererogatory, and the ihram for Umrah. And he indicated that if he returned without ihram and then assumed ihram from it, the blood sacrifice is dropped a fortiori because he initiated the required talbiyah at the beginning of ihram, and for this reason the dropping is agreed upon. And he restricted it by his being in talbiyah at the miqat, because if he returned as a muhrim and did not perform talbiyah at the miqat, the blood sacrifice is not dropped from him, and that is the statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) because he does not make up what he missed except by it. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the blood sacrifice is dropped unconditionally, just as if he assumed ihram from his home and passed the miqats silently, there is nothing upon him by agreement. The answer is that assuming ihram from home is the determination, and he has performed it. So when he takes a concession by delaying to the miqat, it is obligatory upon him to fulfill its right by initiating talbiyah. And he indicated that if he returned as a muhrim and did not perform talbiyah in it, but performed talbiyah after passing it and then returned and passed it silently, then it is dropped a fortiori because it is more than what is obligatory upon him in magnifying the House. And he used the absolute term 'return' so it includes whether he returns to the miqat that he passed without ihram, or to another closer or farther, because all miqats are equal in respect of ihram, and it is better to assume ihram from his own time. Thus in al-Muhit. And we restricted it by his passing the last miqat, as we presented in the chapter of ihram that it is not obligatory except at the last of them, and it is permissible to pass his miqat without ihram if there is another miqat after it. And the author omitted an essential restriction: that the return to the miqat must be before beginning the rituals. So if he returns to it after having performed one circuit (tawaf), it is not dropped. Chapter: Passing the Miqat without Ihram. Indeed, the statement of the author: 'Whoever passes the miqat without ihram...' In al-Nahr it is said: He should have said 'a blood sacrifice is obligatory upon him' but he sufficed with what is understood by implication from his saying 'the blood sacrifice is void'. His statement: 'And what is in al-Hidayah of the restriction by receiving the Black Stone' meaning where he said: If he returns after having begun the circumambulation...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on the chapter 'Passing the Miqat without Ihram'. It explains the ruling that the person who passes the miqat without ihram must offer a dam (blood sacrifice). However, if he returns to the miqat and enters ihram with talbiyah, the dam is waived. The author discusses the difference of opinion between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that performing talbiyah at the miqat is necessary for the dam to be waived, while the Sahibayn hold that simply becoming muhrim at the miqat suffices, even without talbiyah. The author also mentions that the return must be to any miqat (not necessarily the one passed) and must occur before starting the rituals of Hajj or Umrah. The passage ends with a note from al-Nahr criticizing the author's wording and referring to al-Hidayah's restriction regarding touching the Black Stone.

Key Terms
ميقات (miqat): The designated boundary for entering the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
دم (dam): A blood sacrifice (usually a sheep) required as a penalty for missing an obligatory act in Hajj.
تلبية (talbiyah): The declaration 'Labbayk Allahumma Labbayk' recited upon assuming ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the dam to be dropped when a person returns to the miqat after having passed it without ihram?
A) The person must return to the exact same miqat they passed.
B) The person must return and assume ihram with talbiyah before starting the rituals.
C) The person must offer a sacrifice at the miqat.
D) The person must perform two rak'ahs at the miqat.

Key Takeaway
Passing the miqat without ihram obligates a dam, but returning as muhrim with talbiyah at the miqat before the rituals begin drops the obligation; however, Imam Abu Hanifa conditions the drop on talbiyah, while the Sahibayn do not.

The Legal Stratagem (Hila) for Entering Mecca Without Ihram
Ruling on the validity and conditions of the legal stratagem for entering Mecca without ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of the Hanafi legal stratagem for an afaqi to enter Mecca without ihram and explain the dispute between Abu Yusuf and the madhhab regarding the duration of stay.

Core Idea
To enter Mecca without ihram, an afaqi must intend a specific place in the hill as his primary destination — a mere intention to avoid ihram is insufficient.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi legal stratagem that allows a traveler from outside the miqat to enter Mecca without assuming ihram by first intending a place in the hill zone. The passage discusses the conditions for this stratagem and the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the majority of the Hanafi school. Understanding this ruling is essential for applying the principles of intention (niyyah) and legal stratagems (hiyal) correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the legal stratagem (hila) used by an afaqi (non-Makki) to enter Mecca without ihram. The ruling is mubah (permissible) only when specific conditions are met.
The stratagem does not eliminate the obligation of ihram; it only changes the status of the traveler to that of a hill resident.
2
Extract the conditions from the passage
The passage indicates that the traveler must have a genuine primary intention to go to a specific place in the hill (e.g., a garden or town) at the time of crossing the miqat. The intention to enter Mecca must be secondary and incidental, arising after the primary objective.
Without a specific place, the general intention to enter the hill is insufficient.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is based on analogy (qiyas) to residents of the hill, who may enter Mecca for need without ihram. The passage cites al-Bada'i' and Lubab al-Manāsik, which consider the intention at the moment of crossing the miqat as decisive.
The proof is not from direct Quran or hadith but from the reasoning of the Hanafi imams.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf requires that the traveler intend to stay fifteen days in the hill place to sever his traveler status; otherwise, ihram is obligatory. The apparent madhhab (position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) does not impose this condition.
This is a known disagreement within the Hanafi school on the nature of residency (iqamah).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi (operative position) is the madhhab view without the fifteen-day condition, but caution is recommended. Later Hanafi works generally rely on this view for fatwa.
The tarjih authority is the consistent reading of al-Bada'i' and the majority of later scholars.

Source Text

ووقته البستان جميع الحل الذي بينه وبين الحرم قالوا : وهذه حيلة الآفاقي إذا أراد أن يدخل مكة بغير إحرام فينوي أن يدخل خليصاً مثلاً فله مجاوزة رابغ الذي هو ميقات الشامي والمصري المحاذي للجحفة ولم أر أن هـ . فاعتبر الإرادة عند المجاوزة كما ترى ا هـ . أقول : وظاهر ما في البدائع أن من أراد النسك يلزمه الإحرام وإن قصد دخول البستان لقوله «أما إذا لم يرد ذلك الخ» وكذا من يرد الحرم فلا تنفعه إرادة دخول البستان، ويؤخذ ذلك أيضاً من قوله في لباب المناسك : ومن جاوز وقته يقصد مكاناً في الحل ثم بدا له أن يدخل مكة فله أن يدخلها بغير إحرام فقوله ثم بدا له أي ظهر وحدث له يقتضي أنه لو أراد دخول مكة عند المجاوزة يلزمه الإحرام وإن أراد دخول البستان لأن دخول مكة لم يبدله وإنما هو مقصوده الأصلي، وحينئذ يشكل قولهم وهذه حيلة الآفاقي الخ. وقد أشار إلى هذا الإشكال في شرح اللباب ثم قال : والوجه في الجملة أن يقصد البستان قصداً أولياً ولا يضره دخول الحرم بعده قصداً ضمنياً أو عارضياً كما إذا قصد مدني جدة لبيع وشراء أولاً ويكون في خاطره أنه إذا فرغ منه أن يدخل مكة ثانياً بخلاف من جاء من الهند بقصد الحج أولاً ويقصد دخول جدة تبعاً ولو قصد بيعاً وشراء ا هـ . ولا تنس ما مر قبيل باب الإحرام أن من كان داخل المواقيت فميقاته الحل فلا يدخل الحرم عند قصد النسك إلا محرماً، وعليه فمن قصد البستان قصداً أولياً ثم أراد النسك لا يحل له دخول مكة بلا إحرام وانظر ما كتبناه هناك عن الشيخ قطب الدين
So if a Kufan enters a garden for a need, he may enter Mecca without ihram, and his miqat becomes the garden, because he did not initially intend to enter Mecca but intended the garden, so he becomes like its inhabitants when he enters it. And a gardener may enter Mecca without ihram due to need, so likewise for him. What is meant by 'his miqat is the garden' is all the hill (area between miqat and haram) that is between him and the haram. They said: This is a stratagem for the afaqi (person from outside the miqat) if he wants to enter Mecca without ihram: he intends to enter Khulays for example, then he may pass by Rabigh, which is the miqat of the Syrian and Egyptian adjacent to Juhfah. And I have not seen that this intention is necessary from him when he first leaves his home. The apparent view is the first: for there is no doubt that the afaqi intends to enter the hill between the miqat and the haram, and that is not sufficient. So there must be an intention for a specific place in the hill inside the miqat when he leaves his home; otherwise, the apparent view of Abu Yusuf is that if he intends to stay fifteen days in the garden, he may enter Mecca without ihram, otherwise not, but the apparent madhhab is absolute [without restriction]. The intention at the time of crossing is sufficient, and evidence for that is in al-Bada'i' after mentioning the ruling on crossing without ihram; he said: This applies if one crosses one of these five miqats intending hajj or umrah or entry into Mecca or the haram without ihram. But if he does not intend that but rather intends to come to a garden of Banu 'Amir or other for a need, there is nothing upon him. So he considered the intention at the time of crossing, as you see. I say: The apparent meaning of al-Bada'i' is that whoever intends nusk (pilgrimage) is obliged to assume ihram even if he intends to enter a garden, because of his statement 'but if he does not intend that etc.' Similarly, whoever intends the haram, his intention of entering a garden does not benefit him. And that is also taken from his statement in Lubab al-Manāsik: 'Whoever crosses his time intending a place in the hill, then it occurs to him [later] to enter Mecca, he may enter it without ihram.' His phrase 'then it occurs to him' means it appears and happens to him, which implies that if he intended to enter Mecca at the time of crossing, he must assume ihram even if he intended to enter a garden, because entering Mecca did not occur to him later but was his original intention. In this case, their statement 'this is a stratagem for the afaqi' is problematic. And that problem was indicated in Sharh al-Lubab, and then he said: The correct way in summary is that he should intend the garden as a primary intention, and his subsequent entry into the haram as a secondary or incidental intention does not harm him, for example, if a person from Madinah intends Jeddah first for purchase and sale, and in his mind he intends that when he finishes, he will enter Mecca later, unlike one who comes from India with the primary intention of hajj and intends Jeddah as subsidiary, even if he intends purchase and sale. And do not forget what was mentioned before the chapter of ihram: that whoever is inside the miqats, his miqat is the hill, so he should not enter the haram when intending nusk except in a state of ihram. Accordingly, whoever intends the garden as a primary intention, then intends nusk, it is not permissible for him to enter Mecca without ihram. And refer to what we wrote there from Shaykh Qutb al-Din.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage discusses a legal stratagem (hila) for a person from outside the miqat (afaqi) to enter Mecca without assuming ihram. The ruling is that if an afaqi first intends to go to a place in the hal (the area between the miqat and the haram), such as a garden (bustan) or a town like Khulays, he may cross the miqat without ihram and enter Mecca later without ihram, provided his primary intention is that destination. The reasoning is that he becomes like the residents of that place, who are not required to assume ihram for entering Mecca if they do so for a need. The passage notes that the intention to enter the hal alone is insufficient—there must be a specific place in mind when leaving home. Abu Yusuf conditioned this on intending to stay fifteen days in that place; otherwise, the person must assume ihram. However, the apparent position of the madhhab (as in al-Bada'i') is that even a general intention to enter a place in the hal is sufficient, as long as the primary purpose at the time of crossing the miqat is not pilgrimage or entry into the haram. The passage also clarifies that if one's original intention is pilgrimage, the stratagem does not work; the intention to avoid ihram must be secondary and incidental. This is illustrated with the example of a person from Madinah who first goes to Jeddah for trade, then later decides to enter Mecca—he may do so without ihram because his primary intention was Jeddah, not Mecca. In contrast, a person from India who intends hajj primarily cannot avoid ihram by intending Jeddah subsidiary. The passage concludes by warning that even with a primary intention for a garden, if one later intends nusk, he must still assume ihram from the miqat of the hal (i.e., from his place). This ruling is a refined Hanafi position that balances the letter of the law with practical needs, and it is found in the later Hanafi works (fatāwā muta'akhkhirīn).

Key Terms
حيلة (hīlah): A lawful stratagem to achieve a permissible end by indirect means, here to avoid ihram when entering Mecca.
آفاقي (āfāqī): A person who resides beyond the miqat boundaries (outside the hill), i.e., a non-Makki traveler.
حلّ (hill): The area outside the haram of Mecca but inside the miqat boundaries; also called the 'hill zone'.
بستان (bustān): A garden or agricultural land located in the hill; used as an example of a specific destination for the stratagem.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the minimum condition for an afaqi to enter Mecca without ihram using the garden stratagem?
A) He must intend to stay fifteen days in the garden.
B) He must have a genuine primary intention to go to a specific place in the hill (like a garden), not Mecca itself.
C) He must first perform umrah before the stratagem.
D) He must be a resident of the hill originally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi stratagem for entering Mecca without ihram is valid only when the traveler's primary intention at the miqat is a specific place in the hill, not Mecca itself.

The Bostan Stratagem: Entering Mecca without Ihram
Explains the ruling and stratagem for crossing miqat without ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on crossing the miqat without ihram using the bostan stratagem and identify the conditions required for its validity.

Core Idea
A genuine primary intention of the hill lets an afaqi enter Mecca without ihram.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a famous Hanafi stratagem for entering Mecca without ihram. The key is the primacy of intention at the time of crossing the miqat. We will unpack the ruling, the intra-madhhab disagreement, and the precise conditions that make the stratagem valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The text states that a Kufi (someone from within the miqat but outside the Haram) may enter the bostan (a garden in the hill) for a need, and then enter Mecca without ihram. The ruling is based on the analogy with a gardener who may enter Mecca without ihram for a need.
The Kufi's miqat becomes the bostan, meaning he enters the state of ihram from there if he later intends Hajj/Umrah.
2
Locate the primary intention
The stratagem works only if the bostan is the primary intention. If Mecca is the primary goal at the time of crossing the miqat, the blood penalty is due. The text emphasizes that the afaqi must have a genuine, primary intention for a specific place in the hill (like Khulays) before crossing the miqat.
The commentator distinguishes between primary and secondary/incidental intentions.
3
Distinguish the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa holds that no additional condition is needed beyond the genuine intention of the hill at the time of crossing. Abu Yusuf requires an intention to stay in the hill for 15 days for the stratagem to be valid. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's unconditional view.
The text cites al-Bada'i to support the unconditional view.
4
Apply the stratagem correctly
The stratagem fails if the primary intention is to perform Hajj or Umrah, even if one also intends the hill. The correct application: the hill must be the original purpose, and entering Mecca later must be secondary or incidental, like a Madani going first to Jeddah for trade.
If someone from India plans Hajj primarily, intending Jeddah secondarily does not help; the primary intention must be the hill.
5
Note the cross-madhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) do not permit this stratagem. They hold that crossing the miqat without ihram for any reason leading to Mecca requires a sacrifice. The Hanafi response is that the primary intention is genuinely for the hill, making it analogous to residents of the hill.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafis allow entry without ihram; others require ihram from the miqat.

دخل كوفى البستان لحاجة له دخول مكة بغير إحرام ووقته البستان) لأنه لم يقصد أولاً دخول مكة وإنما قصد البستان فصار بمنزلة أهله حين دخله وللبستاني أن يدخل مكة بغير إحرام للحاجة فكذلك له والمراد بقوله Book of Hajj, Chapter on Passing the Miqat without Ihram. The blood [penalty] is nullified. So if a Kufi enters the bostan [garden] for a need, he may enter Mecca without ihram, and his miqat is the bostan. Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary (sharh) on the chapter of passing the miqat without ihram. The author (Ibn Nujaym, the commentator) explains the rule for a person from Kufa who enters a garden (bostan) that lies within the miqat boundary but in the hill area. Since he does not initially intend Mecca, he may enter Mecca without ihram, and his miqat for any subsequent rite becomes that bostan. The passage then discusses a famous Hanafi legal stratagem (hila) for an afaqi to enter Mecca without ihram: he should first intend to go to a specific place in the hill (like Khulays), thereby crossing his miqat without ihram, then later enter Mecca from the hill. The commentator examines different opinions within the madhhab: Abu Hanifa's position is that the mere crossing of the miqat with the intention of the hill is sufficient, while Abu Yusuf requires an explicit intention to stay 15 days in the hill. The author quotes al-Bada'i to support the unconditional view. He also addresses a potential contradiction: if someone's original intention is Mecca, the stratagem fails. The correct application is to make the bostan the primary intention and Mecca secondary. The passage ends by reminding the reader of the earlier rule that those inside the miqat have the hill as their miqat.

Key Terms
البستان (al-bustan): A garden or cultivated area within the hill (al-hill) near Mecca, such as Bani 'Amir's garden.
الآفاقي (al-afaqi): A person coming from outside the miqat boundaries; non-resident of the Haram area.
الحيلة (al-hila): A lawful stratagem to achieve a permissible outcome while technically following the letter of the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The bostan stratagem hinges on making the hill the primary intention; if Mecca is the primary goal, the stratagem is invalid.

Performing Hajj After Entering Mecca Without Ihram: Same Year vs. Later Years
Ruling on validity of Hajj in same year vs. later years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing Hajj in the same year vs. a later year after entering Mecca without ihram, and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas and the concept of dayn).

Core Idea
Hajj in the same year compensates the missed ihram; later years require a specific intention because the obligation becomes a debt.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq addresses a specific scenario: a person enters Mecca without entering ihram at the miqat, then later performs Hajj. The key question is whether the Hajj suffices for the missed obligation if performed in the same year or only if performed later. The Hanafi position distinguishes between the two, based on an analogy with vowed i’tikaf and missed fasts. However, a rebuttal from Fath al-Qadeer challenges this distinction, arguing that no specific year is required. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi jurists treat time-bound obligations that become debts (dayn) when delayed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The passage states that if someone enters Mecca without ihram and then performs Hajj in the same year, it is valid. If the year changes, it is not valid. This is the primary ruling.
The condition is that the Hajj must be performed in the same calendar year as the entry.
2
Understand the rationale: talaafi (compensation in time)
The reason given is that performing Hajj in the same year is 'talaafi' – making up for the omission within its time. The obligation was to honor the sacred precinct by ihram; doing Hajj later fulfills that in the same window.
The analogy is with a vowed i'tikaf: a Ramadan fast fulfills it only in that year.
3
Trace the analogy (qiyas) with dayn (debt)
If the year changes, the obligation becomes a debt (dayn) in the liability. A debt requires a specific intention (niyyah) to discharge, whereas a time-bound obligation can be fulfilled by any act within its period.
The passage analogizes to making up fasts: if you delay, it becomes a debt needing specific qada intention.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi rebuttal from Fath al-Qadeer
Fath al-Qadeer argues that there is no difference between years because the obligation is simply to perform one of the two rites (Hajj or Umrah) whenever done; it is not tied to a specific year. Thus, any later performance is ada' (timely) not qada'.
This rebuttal also applies to repeating the entry multiple times: all crossings can be discharged without specifying which entry.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and practical difference
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that same-year Hajj suffices; later years do not. But the view of Fath al-Qadeer is also considered valid. The practical consequence: if someone delays, they should either perform Hajj in the same year or, if later, specifically intend to discharge the debt.
Al-Usbijabi held a stricter view that each separate crossing requires a separate rite, while Fath al-Qadeer allows combining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول المصنف : (ثم حج عليه في عامه ذلك عبارة الدرر وصح منه لو خرج في عامه ذلك إلى الميقات وأحرم وحج ذلك العام. قال في الشرنبلالية : كذا قيد الخروج إلى الميقات من عامه في الهداية. وفي البدائع ما يقتضى عدم تقييده بالخروج إلى الميقات كما نقله الكمال بقوله : فإن أقام بمكة حتى تحولت السنة (من دخل مكة بلا إحرام ثم حج عما عليه في عامة ذلك دخول مكة صح عن بلا إحرام وإن تحولت السنة لا . قوله : (ومن دخل مكة بلا إحرام ثم حج عما عليه في عام ذلك دخول مكة صح عن بلا إحرام وإن تحولت السنة (لا لأنه تلافي المتروك في وقته لأن الواجب عليه تعظيم هذه البقعة بالإحرام كما إذا أتاها بحجة الإسلام في الابتداء بخلاف ما إذا تحولت السنة لأنه صار ديناً في ذمته فلا يتأدى إلا بإحرام مقصود كما في الاعتكاف المنذور فإنه يتأدى رمضان بصوم هذه السنة دون العام الثاني فإن قلت : سلمنا أن الحجة بتحول السنة تصير ديناً ولكن لا نسلم أن العمرة تصير ديناً لأنها غير مؤقتة قلت : لا شك أن العمرة يكره تركها إلى آخر أيام النحر والتشريق فإذا أخرها إلى وقت يكره صار كالمفوت لها فصارت ديناً. كذا في غاية البيان. وفي فتح القدير : ولقائل أن يقول لافرق بين سنة المجاوزة وسنة أخرى فإن مقتضى الدليل إذا دخلها بلا إحرام ليس إلا وجوب الإحرام بأحد النسكين فقط ففي أي وقت فعل يقع أداء إذ الدليل لم يوجب ذلك في سنة معينة ليصير بفواتها ديناً يقضى، فهما أحرم من الميقات بنسك عليه تأدى هذا الواجب في ضمنه، وعلى هذا إذا تكرر الدخول بلا إحرام ينبغي أن لا يحتاج إلى التعيين وإن كانت أسباباً متعددة الأشخاص دون النوع كما قلنا فيمن عليه يومان من رمضان ينوي مجرد قضاء ما عليه ولم يعين الأول ولا غيره جاز، وكذا لو كانا من رمضانين على الأصح ، وكذا نقول إذا رجع مراراً فأحرم كل مرة بنسك حتى أتى على عدد دخلاته خرج عن عهدة ما عليه .اهـ. يشير إلى رد ما ذكره الأسبيجابي من أنه لو جاوز الميقات قاصداً مكة بلا إحرام مراراً فإنه يجب عليه لكل مرة إما حجة أو عمرة، ولو خرج من عامة ذلك لى الميقات فأحرم بحجة الإسلام أو غيرها فإنه يسقط عنه ما لأجل المجاوزة الأخيرة ولا يسقط عنه ما وجب لأجل مجاوزته قبلها لأن الواجب قبل الأخيرة صار ديناً فلا يسقط إلا بتعيين النية .اهـ. وأطلق المصنف الحج فشمل حجة الإسلام والحجة المنذورة ويلحق به العمرة المنذورة، فلو قال ثم أحرم عما عليه في عامة ذلك لكان أولى ليشمل كل إحرام واجب حجاً أو عمرة، أداء أو قضاء
The author's statement: (Then he performs Hajj due upon him in that year) is the wording of al-Durar. And it is valid if he goes out in that year to the miqat and enters ihram and performs Hajj that year. He said in al-Sharnablaliyyah: Thus the restriction of going out to the miqat in that year is in al-Hidayah. In al-Bada'i, there is what implies no restriction to going out to the miqat, as transmitted by al-Kamal in his statement: For if he stays in Mecca until the year changes ... Whoever enters Mecca without ihram and then performs Hajj due upon him in the year of that entry, it is valid from the entry without ihram. And if the year changes, it is not valid. His statement: (And whoever enters Mecca without ihram, then performs Hajj due upon him in that year of entry, it is valid from the entry without ihram. And if the year changes, it is not) because it is making up for what was omitted in its time, for the obligation upon him is to honor this place by ihram, just as if he came and performed Hajj al-Islam in the beginning. Unlike when the year changes, because it becomes a debt in his liability, and cannot be fulfilled except with an intended ihram, as in the case of a vowed i'tikaf: the Ramadan fast fulfills it in that year, not in the second year. If you say: We grant that the Hajj becomes a debt upon the change of year, but we do not grant that Umrah becomes a debt because it is not time-bound. I say: There is no doubt that it is disliked to delay Umrah until the last days of Nahr and Tashriq; if he delays it to a disliked time, it becomes as if he missed it, so it becomes a debt. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in Fath al-Qadeer: One might say there is no difference between the year of crossing and another year; for the requirement of the evidence is that if he enters it without ihram, only the obligation of ihram with one of the two rites is incumbent. So in whichever time he performs it, it occurs as timely performance (ada'), because the evidence did not obligate it in a specific year such that its passing makes it a debt to be made up. Therefore, if he enters ihram from the miqat with a rite upon him, this obligation is fulfilled within it. And upon this, if he repeatedly enters without ihram, he does not need to specify [which entry] even if the causes are multiple in persons but not in type, as we say in the case of one who owes two days from Ramadan: when he intends merely to make up what is upon him without specifying the first or other, it is valid. Similarly if they are from two Ramadans according to the more correct view. And likewise, if he returns many times and enters ihram each time with a rite until he matches the number of his entries, he is discharged from what is upon him. End. He refers to refuting what al-Usbijabi mentioned: that if one crosses the miqat intending Mecca without ihram multiple times, then for each time he must do either Hajj or Umrah. And if he goes out in that year to the miqat and enters ihram for Hajj al-Islam or other, it only expiates what is due for the last crossing, not what is due for previous ones, because the obligation before the last becomes a debt and cannot be expiated except with specific intention. End. The author [of Kanz al-Daqaiq] used 'Hajj' generically, so it includes Hajj al-Islam and vowed Hajj, and by analogy, vowed Umrah. If he had said 'then he enters ihram for what is upon him in that year' it would have been better to include every obligatory ihram, Hajj or Umrah, ada' or qada'.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling for someone who enters Mecca without ihram and then performs Hajj. The author explains the relied-upon position: if he performs Hajj in the same year he entered, it suffices and compensates for the missed ihram at the miqat. If the year changes, it does not suffice because the obligation has become a debt (dayn) in his liability and requires a specific intention. The commentator then presents a rebuttal from Fath al-Qadeer, which argues that there is no difference between years: the obligation is merely to perform one of the two rites, and whenever performed, it is ada' (timely performance), not qada'. The author notes that the Hanafi master al-Usbijabi held that repeated crossings require separate obligations, but Fath al-Qadeer disagrees, saying that multiple entries can be discharged without specifying which entry, as long as the number of rites matches the number of entries. The passage ends by noting that the text uses 'Hajj' broadly to include all obligatory Hajj and Umrah.

Key Terms
التلافي (al-talaafi): Making up for a missed obligation in its time; compensating for an omission.
الدين في الذمة (al-dayn fi al-dhimmah): A liability that is not tied to a specific time; requires specific intention to discharge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what happens if someone enters Mecca without ihram and performs Hajj in a later year (after the year of entry)?
A) It is valid and no compensation is needed.
B) It is not valid because the obligation becomes a debt.
C) It is valid but a sacrifice is required.
D) It is invalid and must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
Performing Hajj or Umrah in the same year as entering Mecca without ihram compensates the missed obligation; delaying it makes it a debt requiring specific intention.

Hajj — Pilgrimage

Slave's Ihram from Miqat of Mecca after Crossing Miqat without Ihram
Ruling on the slave's ihram after crossing the miqat without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a slave who crosses the miqat without ihram and then is permitted to perform ihram, and identify the two obligations that arise from entering Mecca without ihram.

Core Idea
A slave who enters Mecca without ihram becomes like a resident; his ihram from Mecca's miqat suffices, but he still owes a blood sacrifice for crossing.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hajj rulings: a slave who crosses the miqat without ihram and enters Mecca, then later receives his master's permission to perform the pilgrimage. What are his obligations? Where should he enter ihram? The Hanafi school, drawing on the work al-Muhit, provides a clear answer that balances the slave's situation with the requirements of the rite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and its legal elements
Determine the act: a slave crosses the miqat without ihram, enters Mecca, then his master permits him to perform ihram. Note that two obligations arise: a blood sacrifice (dam) for crossing the miqat without ihram, and the obligation to perform a rite (Hajj or Umrah) for entering Mecca without ihram.
2
Determine the slave's status after staying in Mecca
Because the slave stayed in Mecca, he is considered like its residents. This changes his miqat: for Hajj, the Haram itself; for Umrah, the hill (al-hill).
This principle is based on the reasoning in al-Muhit.
3
State the ruling on where to perform ihram
With the master's permission, the slave may perform ihram from the miqat of the people of Mecca: from the Haram for Hajj and from the hill for Umrah. This suffices for the rite.
He does not need to return to the original miqat he crossed.
4
Analyze the effect of going out to a of miqat
If the slave goes out to a closer miqat (than the one he crossed) and enters ihram, that also suffices. However, entering ihram from within the miqat (i.e., from Mecca) does not remove the blood sacrifice for the original crossing; it only fulfills the rite obligation.
The crossing penalty remains independent.
5
Differentiate the two obligations
The two obligations are separate: (1) dam al-waqt – blood sacrifice for crossing without ihram, and (2) lizum nusuk – obligation of a rite for entering Mecca without ihram. Each has its own ruling and remedy.
The blood sacrifice is removed only if one goes out to a miqat and returns with ihram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثم أذن له مولاه أن يحرم فاحرم أي من مكة. وقوله «لزمه دم الوقت» أي لزمه دم وقال في المحيط : وإذا جاوز العبد ثم أحرم يريد قضاء ما وجب عليه بدخول مكة بغير إحرام أجزأه في ذلك ميقات أهل مكة في الحج بالحرم وبالعمرة بالحل لأنه لما أقام بمكة صار في حكم أهلها فيجزئه إحرامه من ميقاتهم ا هـ . وتعليله يقتضي أن لا حاجة إلى تقييده بتحويل السنة ا هـ. ولو خرج وأهل من ميقات أقرب مما جاوزه أجزأه كما في الفتح عن المبسوط، ثم التقييد بخروجه إلى الميقات يسقط الدم الذي لزمه بمجاوزة الميقات غير محرم بالإحرام منه كما تقدم فإذا أحرم من داخل الميقات لا يسقط عنه المجاوزة لأن المتقرر عليه أمران : دم المجاوزة ولزوم نسك بدخول مكة بلا إحرام، وقد علمت حكم كل فليتنبه ا هـ
His statement: (Then his master permitted him to perform ihram, so he performed ihram) i.e., from Mecca. And his statement: 'He is obligated a blood sacrifice for the time', i.e., he is obligated a blood sacrifice. And it is said in al-Muhit: When a slave crosses [the miqat without ihram], then he performs ihram intending to make up what became obligatory upon him due to entering Mecca without ihram, it suffices for him in that regard the miqat of the people of Mecca: for Hajj in the Haram and for Umrah in the hill, because when he stayed in Mecca, he became like its residents, so his ihram from their miqat suffices. Its reasoning implies that there is no need to restrict it to the change of year. And if he goes out and enters ihram from a miqat closer than what he crossed, it suffices him, as in al-Fath from al-Mabsut. Then the restriction of going out to the miqat only removes the blood sacrifice that became obligatory due to crossing the miqat without ihram, as mentioned earlier. So if he enters ihram from within the miqat, it does not remove the crossing, because what is established upon him are two things: the blood sacrifice for crossing, and the obligation of a rite due to entering Mecca without ihram. You have learned the ruling of each, so be mindful.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a slave who crosses the miqat without ihram and enters Mecca. The text explains that if the master then permits him to perform ihram, the slave may do so from the miqat of the people of Mecca (i.e., from the Haram for Hajj or from the hill for Umrah). This is because his stay in Mecca makes him like a resident. The reasoning implies that the distinction of the year change does not apply to a slave. The passage also clarifies that going out to a closer miqat suffices, but entering ihram from inside the miqat does not remove the blood penalty for the original crossing, because there are two separate obligations: the blood sacrifice for crossing without ihram, and the obligation to perform a rite (Hajj/Umrah) for entering Mecca without ihram.

Key Terms
دم الوقت (dam al-waqt): The blood sacrifice (dam) that becomes obligatory due to crossing the miqat without ihram at the proper time.
ميقات أهل مكة (miqat ahl Makkah): The miqat for the people of Mecca: the Haram for Hajj and the hill for Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a slave enters ihram from within the miqat (i.e., from Mecca) after having crossed the miqat without ihram?
A) All obligations are removed.
B) The blood sacrifice for crossing is removed, but the rite obligation remains.
C) The blood sacrifice for crossing is not removed; the rite obligation is fulfilled.
D) Both obligations are doubled.

Key Takeaway
A slave who crosses the miqat without ihram and later gains permission may perform ihram from the miqat of Mecca, but he still owes a blood sacrifice for the crossing; the two obligations are distinct.

Ruling on Entering Makkah Without Ihram and Delaying Hajj
Clarifying the ruling on one who enters Makkah without ihram and later performs Hajj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on entering Makkah without ihram and apply the distinction between immediate and delayed make-up, including the preferred view on multiple crossings.

Core Idea
Entering Makkah without ihram creates a dual obligation: a blood fine and a ritual to be performed immediately.

This lesson examines a detailed ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the consequences of entering Makkah without ihram from the miqat. The text distinguishes between performing the make-up ritual in the same year versus after the year changes, and explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute about multiple crossings. Understanding this ruling is essential for any Muslim traveling to Makkah for Hajj or Umrah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and initial ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Entering Makkah without ihram) What is the basic hukm? (Obligates blood and a ritual same year valid, otherwise not.)
The text begins with the statement from Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
Understand the 'same year' vs 'year changes' distinction
Why does performing Hajj in the same year suffice but not later? Because the obligation is tied to honoring the sacred place; delay turns it into a debt requiring specific intent.
The analogy to vowed i'tikaf is used.
3
Examine the reasoning for Umrah becoming a debt
The author addresses the objection that Umrah is not time-bound; he explains that delaying it to disliked days makes it like a wasted act, thus a debt.
This comes from Ghayat al-Bayan.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple crossings
Al-Asbijabi held each crossing requires separate intent; the author of Fath al-Qadir holds general intention suffices, analogizing to fasting.
The preferred view in this commentary is the latter.
5
Apply the preferred view and note practical consequences
If someone crosses multiple times without ihram, one Hajj/Umrah with general intention suffices for all, per the chosen position.
This is the mufta bihi according to al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج باب مجاوزة الميقات بغير إحرام بلا إحرام وإن تحولت السنة لا . قوله : (ومن دخل مكة بلا إحرام ثم حج عما عليه في عامة ذلك دخول مكة صح عن بلا إحرام وإن تحولت السنة (لا لأنه تلافي المتروك في وقته لأن الواجب عليه تعظيم هذه البقعة بالإحرام كما إذا أتاها بحجة الإسلام في الابتداء بخلاف ما إذا تحولت السنة لأنه صار ديناً في ذمته فلا يتأدى إلا بإحرام مقصود كما في الاعتكاف المنذور فإنه يتأدى رمضان بصوم هذه السنة دون العام الثاني فإن قلت : سلمنا أن الحجة بتحول السنة تصير ديناً ولكن لا من ذلك منه نسلم أن العمرة تصير ديناً لأنها غير مؤقتة قلت : لا شك أن العمرة يكره تركها إلى آخر أيام النحر والتشريق فإذا أخرها إلى وقت يكره صار كالمفوت لها فصارت ديناً. كذا في غاية البيان. وفي فتح القدير : ولقائل أن يقول لافرق بين سنة المجاوزة وسنة أخرى فإن مقتضى الدليل إذا دخلها بلا إحرام ليس إلا وجوب ينبغي أن لا يحتاج إلى التعيين وإن كانت أسباباً متعددة الأشخاص دون النوع كما قلنا فيمن عليه يومان من رمضان ينوي مجرد قضاء ما عليه ولم يعين الأول ولا غيره جاز، وكذا لو كانا من رمضانين على الأصح ، وكذا نقول إذا رجع مراراً فأحرم كل مرة بنسك حتى أتى على عدد دخلاته خرج عن عهدة ما عليه .اهـ. يشير إلى رد ما ذكره الأسبيجابي من أنه لو جاوز الميقات قاصداً مكة بلا إحرام مراراً فإنه يجب عليه لكل مرة إما حجة أو عمرة، ولو خرج من عامة ذلك لى الميقات فأحرم بحجة الإسلام أو غيرها فإنه يسقط عنه ما لأجل المجاوزة الأخيرة ولا يسقط عنه ما وجب لأجل مجاوزته قبلها لأن الواجب قبل الأخيرة صار ديناً فلا يسقط إلا بتعيين النية .اهـ. وأطلق المصنف الحج فشمل حجة الإسلام والحجة المنذورة ويلحق به العمرة المنذورة، فلو قال ثم أحرم عما عليه في عامة ذلك لكان أولى ليشمل كل إحرام واجب حجاً أو عمرة، أداء أو قضاء أو في المحيط : وإذا جاوز العبد وجب عنه ثم أحرم يريد قضاء ما وجب عليه بدخول مكة بغير إحرام أجزأه في ذلك ميقات أهل مكة في الحج بالحرم وبالعمرة بالحل لأنه لما أقام بمكة صار في حكم أهلها فيجزئه إحرامه من ميقاتهم ا هـ . الأسبيجابي الخ ظاهره اختيار ما بحثه في الفتح مع أنه غير المنقول قوله : (ثم أذن له مولاه أن يحرم فاحرم أي من مكة. وقوله «لزمه دم الوقت» أي لزمه دم الميقات بغير إحرام ثم أذن له مولاه أن يحرم فأحرم لزمه دم الوقت إذا أعتق لأنه من أهل الإحرام فلزمه الإحرام من الميقات وأما الكافر إذا دخل مكة بغير إحرام ثم أسلم فإنه لا يلزمه شيء كالصبي إذا جاوزه بغير إحرام ثم بلغ لعدم أهلية الوجوب . ثم اعلم أنه لا خصوصية للآفاقي في وجوب الدم بترك الإحرام من الميقات، بل المكي كذلك حتى لو أحرم المكي بالعمرة من الحرم فإنه يلزمه دم كما صرح به في المحيط، وكذا لو أحرم المكي من الحل بالحج فإنه يلزمه دم وتتأتى التفاريع المتقدمة في الآفاقي من عوده محرماً ملبياً والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
The Book of Hajj, Chapter: Passing the Miqat without Ihram. His saying: (And whoever enters Makkah without ihram then performs Hajj for what is upon him in that same year of his entry into Makkah, it is valid? Without ihram? And if the year changes, no.) His saying: (No, because he is making up for the omitted act in its time, because the obligation upon him is to honor this sacred place by ihram, just as if he came to it with the Hajj of Islam in the beginning, contrary to if the year changes because it becomes a debt in his liability, so it is not fulfilled except with a deliberate ihram, like in the vowed i'tikaf where Ramadan is fulfilled by fasting that year but not the second year. If you say: We grant that the Hajj by the change of year becomes a debt, but we do not grant that the Umrah becomes a debt because it is not time-bound. I say: There is no doubt that Umrah is disliked to be delayed until the last days of Nahr and Tashreeq, so if he delays it to a disliked time, he becomes like one who has wasted it, so it becomes a debt. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in Fath al-Qadir: One may say there is no difference between the year of crossing and another year, for the purport of the evidence when he enters it without ihram is nothing but the obligation of ihram with one of the two rituals only, so whenever he does it, it falls as performance, because the evidence did not obligate that in a specific year so that its loss becomes a debt to be made up. So if he assumes ihram from the miqat with a ritual that is due on him, this obligation is fulfilled within it. According to this, if the entry without ihram is repeated, he should not need to specify [which entry], even if the causes are multiple in persons but not in type, as we say about one who owes two days of Ramadan: he intends simply to make up what is upon him and does not specify the first or other, it is permissible, and likewise if they are from two Ramadans, on the correct view. And similarly we say: if he returned repeatedly and assumed ihram each time with a ritual until he reaches the number of his entries, he is discharged from what is upon him. End. This indicates a refutation of what al-Asbijabi mentioned, that if he passed the miqat intending Makkah without ihram repeatedly, then for each time he must do either Hajj or Umrah, and if he went out from that same year to the miqat and assumed ihram for the Hajj of Islam or otherwise, then what was due for the last crossing is dropped, but what was due for the crossings before it is not dropped because the obligation before the last became a debt and is not dropped except by specifying the intention. End. And the author [of Kanz] mentioned Hajj absolutely, so it includes the Hajj of Islam and the vowed Hajj, and the vowed Umrah is attached to it. So if he said 'then he assumes ihram for what is upon him in that same year' it would be better to include every obligatory ihram, whether Hajj or Umrah, performance or make-up. Or in al-Muhit: And if a slave passed [the miqat] then he [the slave] was obligated, then he assumed ihram intending to make up what was obligatory upon him due to entering Makkah without ihram, the miqat of the people of Makkah suffices him in Hajj from al-Haram and in Umrah from al-Hill, because when he stayed in Makkah he became like its residents, so his ihram from their miqat suffices him. End. And his reasoning necessitates that there is no need to restrict it to the change of the year. End. And if he went out and performed talbiya from a miqat closer than what he passed, it suffices him, as in al-Fath from al-Mabsut. Then the restriction to his going out to the miqat drops the blood [fine] that was due on him for passing the miqat without ihram, as previously mentioned. So if he assumes ihram from inside the miqat, the [obligation due to] crossing is not dropped because two matters are established upon him: the blood of crossing and the necessary performance of a ritual due to entering Makkah without ihram. You have learned the ruling of each, so pay attention. End. His saying: (It indicates a refutation of what al-Asbijabi mentioned etc.) Apparently it is a choice of what was discussed in al-Fath, although it is not transmitted. His saying: (Then his master permitted him to assume ihram, so he assumed ihram) i.e., from Makkah. And his saying: (He owes the blood of the time) i.e., he owes the blood of the miqat without ihram, then his master permitted him to assume ihram, so he assumed ihram, the blood of the time is due upon him when he is freed because he is an inhabitant of the ihram area, so ihram from the miqat was due upon him. As for the disbeliever, if he entered Makkah without ihram and then accepted Islam, nothing is due upon him, like the minor if he passed it without ihram and then reached puberty, due to lack of capability for obligation. Then know that the non-Makkan is not unique in the obligation of blood for abandoning ihram from the miqat; rather, the Makkan is likewise, so that even if a Makkan assumes ihram for Umrah from al-Haram, he owes blood, as stated in al-Muhit, and likewise if a Makkan assumes ihram from al-Hill for Hajj, he owes blood. And the earlier details for the non-Makkan apply to his returning while in ihram and reciting talbiya. And Allah Most High knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the abode.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the obligation of a person who enters Makkah without ihram. The author begins by stating the ruling: if someone enters Makkah without ihram and then performs Hajj in the same year, it is valid and suffices. However, if the year passes (i.e., he delays Hajj to another year), it does not suffice. The reasoning is that the obligation is to honor the sacred place by assuming ihram from the miqat. If he performs Hajj in the same year, he is making up for the omitted act in its proper time. But if the year changes, the obligation becomes a debt on his liability, and it cannot be fulfilled except by a deliberate ihram with the specific intention of making up for that specific obligation. The author then compares this to a vowed i'tikaf: if one vows to observe i'tikaf in Ramadan, fasting that year fulfills it, but the next year it would not. Then there is a discussion about Umrah: some might argue it does not become a debt because it is not time-bound, but the author counters that delaying Umrah until the disliked days (the last days of Nahr and Tashreeq) makes it like a wasted act, so it becomes a debt. Next, the author quotes from Fath al-Qadir, which presents a different view: there is no distinction between the year of crossing and any other year, because the evidence only obligates ihram with one of the two rituals, and that can be done at any time. According to this view, if one repeatedly enters without ihram, he does not need to specify which entry he is making up for; like one who owes several days of Ramadan, a general intention of making up suffices. The author then notes that this view refutes what al-Asbijabi said, namely that each crossing incurs its own obligation and earlier ones become debts that require specific intent. The author ends by stating that the ruling applies to both the Hajj of Islam and vowed Hajj/Umrah, and also discusses the case of a slave and a disbeliever. A key point: the obligation is two-fold: a blood fine for passing the miqat without ihram, and the obligation to perform a ritual upon entering Makkah. If one assumes ihram from within Makkah, he does not drop the obligation of the ritual; he must still perform a Hajj or Umrah with intent for that specific obligation. The author also clarifies that the ruling applies to Makkans as well: a Makkan who performs Umrah from al-Haram owes blood because he should have gone to al-Hill. This is a nuanced ruling with multiple layers of obligation.

Key Terms
ميقات (miqat): The designated boundary for assuming ihram for Hajj/Umrah.
إحرام (ihram): The state of ritual consecration for Hajj/Umrah, involving specific garments and prohibitions.
دين (dayn): A debt or liability that must be fulfilled by a deliberate act with specific intent.
نسك (nusk): A ritual act of worship, specifically Hajj or Umrah in this context.
دم (dam): A blood sacrifice (e.g., a sheep) required as expiation for certain omissions in Hajj/Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, if someone crosses the miqat without ihram multiple times in different years, then later performs one Hajj with a general intention to make up for any missed obligations, does this suffice?
A) No, because each crossing requires its own specific intent.
B) Yes, a general intention suffices, like making up fasting days.
C) Only if he specifies the most recent crossing.
D) Only if he performs a separate Umrah for each crossing.

Key Takeaway
Entering Makkah without ihram obligates a blood fine and a make-up ritual; the preferred Hanafi view allows a general intention for multiple crossings.

Adding Hajj to an Existing Umrah (Idafat al-Ihram)
Ruling on a Makkan who has started Umrah and then adds Hajj before completing it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a Makkan who combines Hajj and Umrah, explain the consequence of rafd (renunciation) and the obligation of dam (blood sacrifice), and apply the ruling to a scenario.

Core Idea
A Makkan who adds Hajj to an Umrah must renounce the Umrah and owes a blood sacrifice.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario from the Hajj chapter: a Makkan resident who has already commenced an Umrah (by performing at least one tawaf) and then assumes ihram for Hajj. The Hanafi school treats this as a prohibited combination that requires either renouncing the Umrah or continuing with a penalty. We will unpack the ruling, its conditions, and the consequences, as presented in the source text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the person doing? A Makkan, after starting Umrah, adds Hajj by assuming ihram for it. The ruling is that this is prohibited (makruh tahrimi or haram) and incurs a penalty.
The prohibition is specific to Makkans; non-Makkans may combine via tamattu' or qiran.
2
Analyze the two possible outcomes
The text presents two paths: (1) He renounces (rafada) the Umrah, in which case he must perform both Hajj and Umrah separately and pay a dam (blood sacrifice) for the renunciation. (2) He continues with both (i.e., does not renounce), then both are valid but he still owes a dam for violating the prohibition.
The dam is wajib in either case.
3
Understand the legal basis for the penalty
The prohibition stems from the fact that a Makkan is forbidden from combining Hajj and Umrah (tamattu' and qiran). Since he knowingly violated this, he must exit the prohibited state by renouncing or by completing both with a penalty.
The reference to al-Nahr suggests an ikhtilaf about whether the prohibition is on the combination itself or on dissolving the umrah (tamattu').
4
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Consider a Makkan who performs two tawaf of Umrah and then adds Hajj. The ruling is the same: he must renounce the Umrah, perform both separately, and pay the dam.
The number of circuits does not change the ruling as long as the Umrah has been commenced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب إضافة الإحرام إلى الإحرام لما كان ذلك جناية في بعض الصور أو رده عقيب الجنايات قوله : (مكي طاف شوطاً لعمرة فأحرم بحج رفضه وعليه حج وعمرة ودم لرفضه فلو مضى عليهماصح وعليه دم) بيان لحكم الجمع بين الحج والعمرة من المكي فإنه كما قدمناه منهي عن الجمع بينهما، فإذا أدخل إحرام الحج على إحرام العمرة بعد الشروع فيها فقد ارتكب المنهي فوجب عليه الخروج [منه]؟ قوله : (لأنه أدى أفعالهما كما التزمهما الخ قال في النهر : هذا يؤيد قول من قال إن نفي التمتع والقرآن معناه نفي الحل كما مر قول المصنف : (ومن أحرم بحج ثم بآخر) اعلم أن الجميع بين
Chapter: Adding Ihram to Ihram. Because that is an offense in some cases, or its response after offenses. His saying: (A Makkan performed one circuit for Umrah, then assumed ihram for Hajj, he renounces it, and upon him are Hajj and Umrah and a blood fine for renouncing it. If he continues both, they are valid and upon him is a blood fine.) So when he inserts the ihram of Hajj into the ihram of Umrah after having commenced it, he has committed the prohibited act, so it is obligatory upon him to exit from it? Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on adding one ihram to another, specifically for a Makkan. The author clarifies that a Makkan is prohibited from combining Hajj and Umrah (i.e., performing tamattu' or qiran). If a Makkan starts Umrah (e.g., performs one tawaf of Umrah) and then assumes ihram for Hajj, he must renounce (rafada) the Umrah. Consequently, he owes the performance of both Hajj and Umrah separately (since he has invalidated the Umrah by adding Hajj) and also a blood sacrifice (dam) for renouncing the Umrah. If he continues with both (i.e., does not renounce), the acts are valid but he still owes a blood fine as a penalty for combining what is forbidden. The passage then references a discussion from al-Nahr about the meaning of the prohibition of tamattu' and qiran: it indicates that the prohibition is against dissolving the ihram of Umrah before completing it (i.e., tamattu') and not against the combination itself. The text is fragmentary but establishes the basic ruling.

Key Terms
إضافة الإحرام إلى الإحرام (idafat al-ihram ila al-ihram): The act of assuming ihram for one ritual while already in the state of ihram for another.
رفض (rafd): Renouncing or abandoning an ihram that one has already entered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He may continue with both acts without any problem.
D) He must complete the Umrah first and then start Hajj.

Key Takeaway
A Makkan who adds Hajj to an ongoing Umrah must renounce the Umrah, perform both separately, and pay a blood sacrifice.

Ruling for a Makki Who Combines Umrah and Hajj After One Tawaf
Ruling on a Makki who enters Hajj after one tawaf of Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a Makki who combines Umrah and Hajj after one tawaf, and identify the obligations (rejection, separate Hajj and Umrah, dam) based on the scenario.

Core Idea
A Makki who combines Hajj and Umrah must reject the later ihram; failure to reject still valid but requires a dam.

This brief passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a specific scenario unique to the Hanafi school: the prohibition of combining Hajj and Umrah (qiran) for a resident of Makkah. While an Aafaqi (non-resident) may combine, a Makki must choose only one. The ruling addresses what happens when a Makki inadvertently or deliberately enters both ihrams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actor
Determine who is a Makki: a person who resides in Makkah or within the boundaries of the Haram.
2
Recall the prohibition
A Makki is prohibited from combining Hajj and Umrah. The second ihram (Hajj) must be rejected.
3
Recognize the scenario
The Makki has performed one tawaf for Umrah and then entered ihram for Hajj. This triggers the ruling.
The tawaf is not complete; only one circuit.
4
Apply the required action
The Makki must reject the Hajj ihram (rafd) by performing an act of tahallul (e.g., shaving or cutting hair).
Mere intention is insufficient; a specific action is needed.
5
State the consequences
After rejection, he owes a separate Hajj, a separate Umrah, and a dam (sacrifice). If he continues with both (does not reject), the combination is valid but he owes a dam for the prohibited combination.
The dam is wajib in both scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكي طاف شوطاً لعمرة فأحرم بحج رفضه وعليه حج وعمرة ودم لرفضه فلو مضى عليهما صح وعليه دم
A Makki who performed one tawaf for Umrah and then entered Ihram for Hajj rejects it (the Hajj), and upon him is (the obligation of) Hajj and Umrah and a dam for his rejection. If he continues with both, it is valid and upon him is a dam.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) regarding a resident of Makkah who begins Umrah, performs one tawaf, and then enters into the state of ihram for Hajj. Since the Makki is prohibited from combining Hajj and Umrah (as per the Hanafi school), he must reject the Hajj. Upon rejection, he owes both a Hajj and an Umrah (to be performed separately) and a dam (sacrifice) for the rejection. If he instead continues with both, his actions are valid but he owes a dam for the prohibited combination. This ruling is specific to the Makki; for the Aafaqi (one from outside), the ruling differs.

Key Terms
مكي (Makki): A person who is a resident of Makkah or within the boundaries of the Haram.
رفض (rafd): To abandon or reject an ihram by performing an act of tahallul (breaking the ihram).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He owes nothing.
D) He owes only a Hajj.

Key Takeaway
A Makki who combines Umrah and Hajj must reject the later ihram; the combination is valid but requires a dam if not rejected.

Exceptions to Dam for Crossing Miqat Without Ihram
Rulings on exceptions to dam for crossing miqat without ihram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on crossing the miqat without ihram for a slave, disbeliever, and child, and apply the principle of ahliyyah to determine when dam is wajib.

Core Idea
Dam for crossing miqat depends on capacity at the time of crossing.

In this lesson, we explore exceptions to the general rule that crossing the miqat without ihram requires a dam. The text examines three categories—slave, disbeliever, and child—showing that obligation hinges on whether the person was from 'ahl al-ihram' at the time of crossing. It also clarifies that the ruling applies to Makkis as well.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each category
Read the text and note the specific rulings for the slave, disbeliever, and child. What is required of each?
2
Understand the underlying principle
The key concept is 'ahliyyah' (capacity for obligation). Why are the disbeliever and child exempt, while the slave owes a dam when freed?
The slave later gains capacity by being freed.
3
Apply the principle to new cases
Suppose a child crosses the miqat without ihram and then reaches puberty before entering Makkah. Does he owe a dam? Use the principle from the text.
4
Note the extension to Makkis
The text states that the ruling is not limited to those from outside Makkah. What example does it give?
A Makki entering ihram for Umrah from the Haram or for Hajj from the Halal also owes a dam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو جاوز الميقات بغير إحرام ثم أذن له مولاه أن يحرم فأحرم لزمه دم الوقت إذا أعتق لأنه من أهل الإحرام فلزمه الإحرام من الميقات وأما الكافر إذا دخل مكة بغير إحرام ثم أسلم فإنه لا يلزمه شيء كالصبي إذا جاوزه بغير إحرام ثم بلغ لعدم أهلية الوجوب . ثم اعلم أنه لا خصوصية للآفاقي في وجوب الدم بترك الإحرام من الميقات، بل المكي كذلك حتى لو أحرم المكي بالعمرة من الحرم فإنه يلزمه دم كما صرح به في المحيط، وكذا لو أحرم المكي من الحل بالحج فإنه يلزمه دم وتتأتى التفاريع المتقدمة في الآفاقي من عوده محرماً ملبياً والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
If he crosses the miqat without ihram, then his master permits him to enter ihram and he does so, he is liable for the dam of the time when he is freed, because he is among the people of ihram, so ihram from the miqat became obligatory upon him. As for the disbeliever who enters Makkah without ihram and then becomes Muslim, nothing is required of him, like the child who crosses it without ihram and then reaches puberty, due to lack of capacity for obligation. Then know that there is no specificity for the Aafaqi in the obligation of dam for leaving ihram from the miqat; rather, the Makki is similar. So if a Makki enters ihram for Umrah from the Haram, then a dam is required of him, as clarified in al-Muhit. Similarly, if a Makki enters ihram for Hajj from the Halal, then a dam is required of him. And the previously mentioned branches for the Aafaqi apply regarding his returning in ihram and in talbiyah. Commentary
This passage continues the rulings on crossing the miqat without ihram. It addresses three categories: a slave, a disbeliever, and a child. For the slave who crosses the miqat without ihram and then is permitted by his master to enter ihram, he owes a dam when he is freed. The reason is that at the time of crossing, he was not obligated due to lack of capacity, but once he acquires capacity (by becoming free), the obligation attaches. For the disbeliever who later converts to Islam, no dam is required because at the time of crossing he was not from the people of obligation. Similarly for the child who later attains puberty. The passage then clarifies that the ruling of wajib dam for crossing the miqat without ihram is not specific to the Aafaqi (one from outside Makkah); it also applies to the Makki if he enters ihram from within the Haram for Umrah or from the Halal for Hajj. This is a general principle.

Key Terms
أهل الإحرام (ahl al-ihram): Those who are capable and obligated to perform ihram at the miqat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Dam for crossing miqat without ihram is only required from those who were from ahl al-ihram at the time of crossing; slaves, disbelievers, and children are exempt until they gain capacity.
