Dam Obligations for Ihsaar After Standing at Arafah
Commentary on the matn: no ihsaar after standing at Arafah, with dam obligations and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on dam obligations for a muhsir after standing at Arafah and distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A muhsir after standing at Arafah owes four dam according to Abu Hanifa, but only two according to the Sahibayn.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that the concept of ihsaar does not apply after standing at Arafah because the Hajj cannot be lost. However, if a person is prevented from continuing the rites, he must compensate for missed wajibat. The passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute about the number of blood offerings (dam) required when the prevention continues through the days of Tashreeq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'no ihsaar after standing at Arafah'
The phrase means that once the essential pillar (standing at Arafah) is performed, the Hajj cannot be lost (fatat), so the concept of ihsaar (prevention that necessitates a sacrifice) does not apply. The pilgrim is safe from loss but still must complete other rites.
This is because the rukn of Hajj is already done.
2
Identify the four wajibat that may be missed and require compensation
According to Abu Hanifa, if prevention continues, the person owes a dam for each of the following: leaving the standing at Muzdalifah, leaving the stoning of the pillars, delaying shaving, and delaying the visitation tawaf.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that each of these four missed wajibat requires a separate dam, because they are obligations related to the rites of Hajj and no excuse (even enemy-caused prevention) absolves from compensation.
The source states: 'فعليه لترك الوقوف بالمزدلفة دم، ولترك الجمار دم، ولتأخير الحلق دم، ولتأخير الطواف دم في قول أبي حنيفة.'
4
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that only the first two (Muzdalifah and stoning) require a dam; delaying shaving and tawaf does not require any sacrifice because these acts can be performed later without a missed obligation.
They argue that delay due to an excuse does not necessitate a dam.
5
Analyze the commentator's harmonization
The commentator notes that the dispute should be understood based on the cause of ihsaar: if caused by an enemy (human agency), the Sahibayn's position (no dam for shaving/tawaf) applies; if caused by illness (divine), no dam at all. This restricts the application of Abu Hanifa's view.
The commentator says: 'ظهر لي أن كلامهم هنا محمول على الإحصار بسبب العدو لا مطلقاً.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا) إحصار بعدما وقف بعرفة لأنه لا يتصور الفوات بعده فأمن منه، يتوقت قوله : (وجوابه أن الإحصار عرفة ليس بإحصار الخ) دفعه في النهر بأن منشأ اعتراضه التحريف لأن النسخة لو أحصر بعرنة بالنون وإلا فكيف يصح أن يكون بحيث يدرك الحج قوله : (فكفر الموكل) ظاهره أنه قيد لصحة كون ما في يد الوكيل كفارة لليمين الثانية بسبب عدم الوجوب للأولى ومقتضى قوله وكذا لو بعث هدياً عدم التقييد تأمل قول المصنف : (ولا إحصار بعد ما وقف بعرفة) اعترضه بعضهم بأنه تكرار محض مع ما يأتي من قوله ومن منع بمكة الخ قوله : (وقد ظهر لي الخ) نقله عنه في النهر وأقره عليه وكان الشرنبلالي لم يقف على ما هنا فاستشكل المسألة أيضاً وفي وإنما تحقق الإحصار في العمرة وإن كانت لا تفوت للزوم الضرر بامتداد الإحرام فوق ما التزمه، وأما المحصر في الحج بعد الوقوف فيمكنه التحلل بالحلق يوم النحر في غير النساء فلا ضرورة إلى التحلل بالدم. ثم إن دام الإحصار حتى مضت أيام التشريق فعليه لترك الوقوف بالمزدلفة دم، ولترك الجمار دم، ولتأخير الحلق دم، ولتأخير الطواف دم في قول أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد ليس عليه لتأخير الحلق والطواف شيء. كذا في الكافي للحاكم الشهيد، وقد قدمنا عن البدائع وغيره أن واجب الحج إذا تركه بعذر لا شيء عليه حتى لو ترك الوقوف بالمزدلفة خوف الزحام لا شيء عليه كما لا شيء على الحائض بترك طواف الصدر فلا شك أن الإحصار عذر فلا شيء عليه يترك الواجبات للعذر مع أنه منقول في الحاكم كما رأيت وهو جمع كلام محمد في كتبه الستة التي هي ظاهر الرواية. وقد ظهر لي أن كلامهم هنا محمول على الإحصار بسبب العدو لا مطلقاً فإنه إذا كان بالمرض فهو سماوي يكون عذراً في ترك الواجبات وإن كان من قبل العباد فإنه لا يكون عذراً في إسقاط حق الله تعالى كما قالوه في باب التيمم أن العدو إذا أسروا حتى صلى بالتيمم فإنه يعيدها بالوضوء إذا أطلق لأنه من قبل العباد ثم اختلفوا في تحلل المحصر بعد الوقوف؛ قيل لا يتحلل في مكانه ويدل عليه عبارة الأصل حيث قال : وهو حرام كما هو حتى يطوف الزيارة. وهو يدل على تأخير الحلق على أن يفعله في الحرم. وقيل يتحلل في مكانه ويدل عليه عبارة الجامع الصغير حيث قال: وهو محرم على النساء حتى يطوف طواف الزيارة. قال العتابي: وهو الأظهر. كذا في غاية البيان
He said: (And there is no) ihsaar after he has stood at Arafah because the Fat (loss of Hajj) cannot be conceived after it, so he is safe from it. It is time-bound. He said: (And its answer is that ihsaar at Arafah is not ihsaar...) He refuted it in al-Nahr that the source of his objection is distortion, because if the text says 'he was prevented at 'Arana' with a noon, otherwise how could it be that he can reach the Hajj? He said: (And the expiation of the principal) apparently it is a condition for the validity of what is in the agent's hand being expiation for the second oath due to the lack of obligation for the first. And the implication of his statement 'and similarly if he sends a hady' is the lack of restriction. Consider the author's statement: (And there is no ihsaar after standing at Arafah) some objected that it is a pure repetition with what will come of his statement 'and whoever is prevented at Makkah from the two pillars'. He said: (And it has become clear to me...) He transmitted it from him in al-Nahr and approved it, and al-Shurunbulali did not come across what is here, so he raised an issue about the matter as well. And [on page 100:] Indeed, ihsaar is realized in Umrah, even though it is not subject to Fat, because of the necessity of harm due to extension of ihram beyond what he committed. As for the muhsir in Hajj after standing, he can perform tahallul by shaving on the Day of Nahr, except in the case of women, so there is no need for tahallul with blood. Then, if the ihsaar continues until the days of Tashreeq pass, he owes a dam for leaving the standing at Muzdalifah, a dam for leaving the stoning, a dam for delaying shaving, and a dam for delaying tawaf according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: He owes nothing for delaying shaving and tawaf. Thus in al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid. And we have already presented from al-Bada'i' and others that if a wajib of Hajj is left due to an excuse, nothing is owed. Even if one leaves the standing at Muzdalifah due to fear of crowds, nothing is owed, just as a menstruating woman owes nothing for leaving the farewell tawaf. So there is no doubt that ihsaar is an excuse, so he owes nothing for leaving the wajibat due to the excuse, while it is transmitted in al-Hakim as you saw, and it is a collection of Muhammad's statements in his six books which are Zahir al-Riwayah. And it has become clear to me that their statement here is restricted to ihsaar caused by an enemy, not absolutely. For if it is due to illness, it is divine and is an excuse for leaving the wajibat. And if it is from the side of humans, it is not an excuse for dropping the rights of Allah, as they said in the chapter of Tayammum that if enemies capture [someone] so that he prays with tayammum, he must repeat it with wudu when released, because it is from the side of humans. Then they differed about the tahallul of the muhsir after standing: Some said he does not perform tahallul in his place, and this is indicated by the expression of the original text where he said: 'He is in ihram as he is until he performs the visitation tawaf.' This indicates delaying shaving so that he does it in the Haram. And some said he performs tahallul in his place, and this is indicated by the expression of al-Jami' al-Saghir where he said: 'He is forbidden to women until he performs the visitation tawaf.' Al-'Attabi said: This is the more apparent. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase 'there is no ihsaar after standing at Arafah.' The reason is that after standing at Arafah, the Hajj cannot be 'lost' (fatat) because the essential act has been performed. Therefore, the concept of ihsaar (prevention) does not apply. However, if someone is prevented from completing the remaining rites after standing, he is not a muhsir in the technical sense, but he still must fulfill obligations. The passage then details the blood offerings (dam) required if the ihsaar continues through the days of Tashreeq: for missing the standing at Muzdalifah, for missing the stoning, for delaying shaving, and for delaying tawaf. According to Abu Hanifa, each of these requires a separate dam. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that no dam is required for delaying shaving or tawaf. The commentator harmonizes this with the general principle that missing a wajib due to an excuse (like illness or enemy) does not require dam, by noting that the ruling about dam applies only to ihsaar caused by an enemy (human agency), not by illness (divine). Finally, there is a difference of opinion about whether a muhsir after standing can perform tahallul at his location or must wait to do so in the Haram. The commentator presents two views, with al-'Attabi preferring the view that he can perform tahallul in his place.

Key Terms
الوقوف بعرفة (al-wuquf bi 'Arafah): The essential pillar of Hajj: standing at Arafah on the 9th of Dhul Hijjah.
الوقوف بالمزدلفة (al-wuquf bi al-Muzdalifah): Standing at Muzdalifah on the night of the 10th, a wajib of Hajj.
الجمار (al-jimar): The stoning of the three pillars at Mina, a wajib of Hajj.
الحلق (al-halq): Shaving the head after completing the major rites, a wajib for exiting ihram.
طواف الزيارة (tawaf al-ziyarah): The obligatory tawaf after standing at Arafah and before shaving, also called tawaf al-ifadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many blood offerings (dam) are required for a muhsir in Hajj who remains prevented after standing at Arafah through the days of Tashreeq?
A) One dam for each missed wajib: standing at Muzdalifah, stoning, shaving, and tawaf.
B) One dam for missing standing at Muzdalifah and one for stoning.
C) Only one dam for all missed wajibat.
D) No dam at all because ihsaar is an excuse.

Key Takeaway
The number of dam for a muhsir after standing at Arafah depends on which Hanafi imam is followed: Abu Hanifa requires four, the Sahibayn require two, and the cause (enemy vs. illness) may further affect the ruling.

The Ruling of Ihsaar at Makkah: Prevention from Both Pillars
Commentary on the matn defining ihsaar at Makkah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who is considered a muhsir at Makkah and state the practical consequence of the distinction between human-caused and divine-caused prevention.

Core Idea
Only prevention from both tawaf and sa'ee at Makkah constitutes ihsaar; the obligation of dam depends on the cause's origin.

This commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the specific ruling of ihsaar for someone at Makkah. The phrase 'منع بمكة عن الركنين' (prevented from both pillars) sets a precise threshold: if a person can perform even one pillar, they are not a muhsir. The commentator then addresses a deeper legal question: can fear of crowds (a human-related cause) exempt one from the dam? The answer involves a subtle distinction between human-agency and divine decree, with implications for the obligation of compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from the matn
Read the matn phrase: 'ومن منع بمكة عن الركنين فهو محصر وإلا لا'. State clearly: a person is considered muhsir only if prevented from both tawaf and sa'ee. If able to do one, he is not muhsir.
The two pillars are tawaf and sa'ee, though in Hanafi law sa'ee is actually wajib, not a rukn.
2
Understand the reasoning for the ruling
The commentator explains: if prevented from both in the Haram, completion (itmam) becomes impossible, similar to ihsaar outside the Haram. If one can do tawaf, tahallul is possible through it, and the dam is a substitute for tawaf in tahallul.
3
Analyze the dispute about the cause of prevention
The passage mentions a dispute (apparently between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) about whether leaving a wajib due to an excuse requires dam. It distinguishes between human-caused prevention (enemy) and divine causes (illness).
The text is garbled but the principle is clear from the subsequent discussion.
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction about fear of crowds
An objection is raised: if human-caused prevention requires dam, why is there no dam for leaving Muzdalifah due to fear of crowds? The commentator resolves: fear of crowds does not stem from a direct human cause, so it is attributed to Allah and is excused.
This distinction relies on the cause: fear from an enemy's threat is human-caused; fear of crowds is a general divine-created fear.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Consider two scenarios: pilgrim prevented from both tawaf and sa'ee by an enemy (human cause) → he is muhsir and must send hady. Pilgrim prevented by sudden illness (divine cause) → he is muhsir but no dam required (according to the preferred view).
The matn does not specify the cause, but the commentary provides the nuance.

قوله : (ومن منع بمكة عن الركنين فهو محصر وإلا لا) أي وإن قدر على أحدهما فليس بمحصر لأنه إذا منع عنهما في الحرم فقد تعذر عليه الإتمام فصار كما إذا أحصر في الحل، وإذا قدر على الطواف فلأن فائت الحج يتحلل به والدم بدل عنه في التحلل، وأما إن قدر على الوقوف فلما بينا . وقد قيل في المسألة خلاف بين أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف؟ (the text says 'أبي' then continues) الرمز للمقدسي ومزان ترك واجب الحج لعذر لا شيء فيه وهو محمول على ما يكون بعدو وأما المرض فسماوي يعذر به ا هـ وقدمنا مثله عن شرح اللباب عند قول المصنف في الجنايات أو ترك السعي قوله : (وإن كان من قبل العباد فإنه لا يكون عذر الخ إن قلت ينافي هذا الحمل ما ذكره من عدم وجوب شيء بترك الوقوف بمزدلفة خوف الزحام فقد جعلوه عذراً مع أنه من قبل العباد كالخوف من العدو في التيمم قلت قد مر هناك الاختلاف في أن الخوف من العدو من الله أو من العباد والذي حققه المؤلف هناك وصرح به ابن أمير حاج أنه إن حصل بسبب وعيد من قبل العباد وإلا فمن الله تعالى فإن الخوف مطلقاً وإن كان منه تعالى خلقاً وإرادة لكن لما استند إلى مباشرة سبب من العبد أضيف إليه وما هنا لم يحصل عن مباشرة سبب له فكان مسند إليه تعالى. العبد فهو من
He said: (And whoever is prevented at Makkah from the two pillars, he is a muhsir; otherwise not.) That is, if he is able to [perform] one of them, he is not a muhsir, because if he is prevented from both in the Haram, then completion has become impossible for him, so he becomes like one who is prevented in the Hal (outside the Haram). If he is able to perform tawaf, then because the one who missed the Hajj can perform tahallul through it, and the blood offering is a substitute for it in tahallul. And if he is able to stand [at Arafah], then as we have explained. And it has been said about the matter that there is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu... [unclear] 'al-Ramz al-Maqdisi wa Mizan' Leaving a wajib of Hajj due to an excuse, there is no obligation for it, and it is restricted to what is due to an enemy. As for illness, it is divine and is excused. We have presented similar from Sharh al-Lubab at the author's statement in the chapter of crimes or leaving the sa'ee. He said: (And if it is from the side of humans, then it is not an excuse...) If you say: This context contradicts what he mentioned that there is no obligation for leaving the standing at Muzdalifah due to fear of crowds, for they considered it an excuse even though it is from the side of humans, like fearing the enemy in tayammum. I say: The difference has already been discussed: that fear of the enemy is from Allah or from humans. And what the author verified there and Ibn Amir Hajj stated explicitly is that if it occurs due to a threat from humans, then it is from them; otherwise it is from Allah. For fear, even if it is created by Allah and willed by Him, is attributed to the human when it is based on a direct cause from him. And here [the fear of crowds] does not occur due to a direct cause from the human, so it is attributed to Allah. So the human is from [the one who caused it?]
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase: 'Whoever is prevented at Makkah from the two pillars (tawaf and sa'ee) is a muhsir; otherwise not.' The commentator explains that if a person is prevented from performing both tawaf and sa'ee within the Haram, he is considered a muhsir because he cannot complete his Hajj or Umrah. If he is able to perform one of them, he is not a muhsir, because he can still achieve tahallul through the one he can perform. The blood offering (dam) is a substitute for tawaf if he misses it. The passage then goes into a discussion about the distinction between excuses caused by humans (like enemies) and divine causes (like illness). It addresses an objection about the contradiction between the statement that missing a wajib due to an excuse requires no dam, and the earlier statement that the muhsir by enemy must offer dam. The resolution is that fear of crowds is not caused by a direct human cause, so it is attributed to Allah and thus exempts from dam. The passage ends with an incomplete statement about the human's role.

Key Terms
الركنين (al-ruk'nayn): The two pillars: tawaf and sa'ee (in the context of Hajj/Umrah at Makkah).
الحرم (al-Haram): The sacred precinct of Makkah and the Kaaba.
الصدر (al-sadr): The farewell tawaf (tawaf al-wada'), which is wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, what constitutes ihsaar for someone at Makkah?
A) Being prevented from any single pillar of Hajj.
B) Being prevented from both tawaf and sa'ee.
C) Being prevented only from standing at Arafah.
D) Being prevented from all rites due to illness.

Key Takeaway
The definition of ihsaar at Makkah is narrowly limited to prevention from both tawaf and sa'ee, and the obligation of dam depends on whether the cause is human (required) or divine (exempted).

Blood Sacrifices for the Muhsar: Enemy vs. Illness
Commentary on the intra-Hanafi dispute over blood sacrifices for missed wajibat due to ihsar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on blood sacrifices for missed wajibat due to ihsar and apply the distinction between prevention by an enemy (not excused) and by illness (excused).

Core Idea
A muhsar's penalty depends on the cause of prevention: illness excuses, enemy does not.

When a pilgrim is prevented from completing Hajj or Umrah after entering ihram, does he owe blood sacrifices for every missed wajib? Abu Hanifa and his two students disagree. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq reconciles their positions by focusing on the cause of prevention: illness (heavenly) makes leaving wajibat excusable, while prevention by an enemy (human cause) does not. In this lesson, you will dissect this ruling and learn how to apply the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The muhsar (prevented pilgrim) who misses certain wajibat after the Days of Tashreeq. Determine the hukm level of the blood sacrifices.
The blood sacrifices are wajib, not fard, because the missed acts are wajibat of Hajj, not pillars.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
State Abu Hanifa's position: separate blood sacrifice for each missed/delayed act (standing at Muzdalifah, stoning, shaving, Tawaf). Then state the Sahibayn's position: nothing due for delaying shaving and Tawaf.
The disagreement is only about delaying shaving and Tawaf; other missed acts are agreed upon.
3
Follow the Hanafi response to the ikhtilaf
The author reconciles by distinguishing the cause of prevention. For prevention by an enemy (from the deeds of servants), penalties apply. For prevention by illness (heavenly), the muhsar is excused and nothing is due.
This distinction is supported by analogy with tayammum and the principle that rights of Allah are not nullified by human acts.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Although Abu Hanifa's view is in Zahir al-Riwayah, the author leans toward the Sahibayn by restricting the ruling to enemy-caused prevention. The mufta bihi today is that no blood sacrifice is due for delaying shaving and Tawaf if the prevention is due to illness.
The tarjih is based on the excuse principle affirmed in al-Bada'i' and other sources.
5
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Given a scenario where a muhsar is prevented by a government closure (human cause) vs. sudden illness, determine whether blood sacrifices are required for missed wajibat.
Government closure is akin to enemy prevention; illness is heavenly.

And indeed ihsar is realized in Umrah, even though it cannot be missed, due to the necessity of harm by extending the ihram beyond what one committed to. As for the one prevented in Hajj after standing (at Arafah), he can exit ihram by shaving on the Day of Sacrifice, except for women, so there is no need to exit by offering blood. Then if the prevention continues until the days of Tashreeq pass, then for missing standing at Muzdalifah he owes a blood sacrifice, for missing the stoning a blood sacrifice, for delaying shaving a blood sacrifice, and for delaying Tawaf a blood sacrifice according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Nothing is due for delaying shaving and Tawaf. This is in al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid. We have previously mentioned from al-Bada’i‘ and others that if a wajib of Hajj is left due to an excuse, nothing is due. Even if one misses standing at Muzdalifah due to fear of crowding, nothing is due, just as nothing is due on a menstruating woman for missing the farewell Tawaf. So there is no doubt that ihsar is an excuse, so nothing is due for leaving wajibat due to an excuse, and it is transmitted in al-Hakim as you have seen, and it is a compilation of Muhammad’s statements in his six books which are Zahir al-Riwayah. And it has become apparent to me that their speech here is restricted to ihsar caused by an enemy, not absolutely. For if it is due to illness, it is heavenly and is an excuse for leaving wajibat. But if it is from the deeds of servants, then it is not an excuse for nullifying a right of Allah, as they said in the chapter of Tayammum: if an enemy captures them and they pray with Tayammum, then if released, they must repeat with ablution because it was due to servants. Then they differed concerning the tahallul of the muhsar after standing. It is said: He does not perform tahallul in his place, and the wording of al-Asl indicates it: 'He remains in ihram as he is until he performs Tawaf al-Ziyarah.' This indicates delaying shaving until he does it in the Haram. And it is said: He performs tahallul in his place, and the wording of al-Jami‘ al-Saghir indicates it: 'He is forbidden for women until he performs Tawaf al-Ziyarah.' Al-‘Attabi said: This is more apparent. So in Ghayat al-Bayan. His statement: (Whoever is prevented in Makkah from the two pillars is a muhsar, otherwise not). That is, if he is able to perform one of them, he is not a muhsar, because if prevented from both in the Haram, completing (Hajj) becomes difficult, so it is like being prevented in the Halal. If he is able to perform Tawaf, then because the one who misses Hajj exits ihram through it, and blood is its substitute in tahallul. But if he is able to stand (at Arafah), it is as we explained. Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the rulings of ihsar (prevention) in Hajj and Umrah. The author clarifies that ihsar is realized in Umrah even though it cannot be 'missed' (fawat) because the harm of extending ihram beyond what was intended necessitates a means of exit. For the one prevented in Hajj after standing at Arafah, he can exit ihram by shaving on the Day of Sacrifice (except women), so there is no need for a blood sacrifice for tahallul. However, if the prevention continues past the Days of Tashreeq, then according to Abu Hanifa, separate blood sacrifices are due for missing: standing at Muzdalifah, stoning, and delaying both shaving and Tawaf. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree, holding that nothing is due for delaying shaving and Tawaf. The author then reconciles the positions by citing the principle that leaving a wajib due to an excuse absolves one of penalty, as in the case of fear of crowds at Muzdalifah. He further distinguishes between prevention by an enemy (which is not a full excuse) and by illness (which is 'heavenly' and a valid excuse). The passage also discusses tahallul after standing: whether the muhsar can shave in his place or must wait until performing Tawaf al-Ziyarah in the Haram. Finally, the commentary on the matn 'Whoever is prevented in Makkah from the two pillars (Tawaf and standing) is a muhsar' explains that being able to perform one of them negates ihsar status. The author concludes by addressing an apparent contradiction regarding fear of crowds being considered an excuse despite originating from servants, resolving it by tracing the cause to Allah if no direct human threat exists. This lesson is a detailed treatment of ihsar with intra-madhhab disputes and usuli reasoning.

Key Terms
الإحصار (al-ihsar): Prevention from completing Hajj or Umrah due to enemy, illness, or other forces beyond one's control.
المحصر (al-muhsar): One who is prevented from completing Hajj or Umrah after having entered ihram.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, which are the most authoritative sources of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both excuse all penalties equally.
B) Illness is a heavenly excuse and negates penalties; enemy prevention does not.
C) Enemy prevention is a greater excuse and negates penalties; illness does not.
D) Neither excuses penalties; blood sacrifices are always required.

Key Takeaway
A muhsar's obligation for blood sacrifices depends on the cause of prevention: enemy-caused prevention requires sacrifices for missed wajibat; illness-caused prevention excuses delays in shaving and Tawaf.

The Four Rulings of Fawat (Missing Hajj)
Commentary on the four rulings of fawat with evidence and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four rulings of fawat (missing Hajj) in Hanafi fiqh and explain the evidence for each, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of the exit.

Core Idea
Missing Hajj means missing Wuquf at Arafah: exit with Umrah, repeat next year, but no blood sacrifice.

When a pilgrim misses the essential standing at Arafah before its time expires, the Hajj is not lost in vain. The Hanafi school provides a clear set of four rulings based on ijma‘ and hadith. This lesson unpacks these rulings, their proofs, and a subtle disagreement among the Hanafi imams regarding how the pilgrim exits ihram.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the matn
Read the matn (the original text) and identify the four distinct rulings it contains. Each ruling corresponds to a specific legal obligation or concession.
The matn states: 'Whoever misses Hajj by missing the standing at Arafah, then let him exit with Umrah, and upon him is Hajj next year without blood.'
2
Verify the evidence for the first three rulings
The text explicitly states that there is no disagreement among the Ummah on these three rulings. This means they are proven by ijma‘ (consensus). Note that ijma‘ is a primary source of law.
The three rulings are: (1) fawat is only by missing Wuquf, (2) must exit with Umrah actions, (3) must make up the Hajj.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence for the fourth ruling
The fourth ruling (no blood sacrifice) is based on a hadith from al-Daraqutni. Assess its strength: the text notes it is weak (da‘if) but becomes hasan due to multiple chains. Understand how Hanafi usul accepts such hadith when no contradicting evidence exists.
The hadith text is not directly quoted, but its meaning indicates that no blood is required for fawat.
4
Resolve the intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of exit
The commentary states that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the ihram of Hajj does not transform into an ihram of Umrah; rather, the actions of Umrah simply release the Hajj ihram. Abu Yusuf held that it transforms. Compare the two views and note the evidence from the case of the qarin (one who combines Hajj and Umrah).
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الفوات
قوله : (ومن فاته الحج بفوت الوقوف بعرفة فليحل بعمرة وعليه الحج من قابل بلا دم) بيان لأحكام أربعة : الأول أن قوات الحج لا يكون إلا بفوت الوقوف بعرفة بمضي وقته. الثاني أنه إذا فاته يجب عليه أن يخرج منه بأفعال العمرة الثالث لزوم القضاء سواء كان ما شرع فيه حجة الإسلام أو نذراً أو تطوعاً، ولا خلاف بين الأمة في هذه الثلاثة فدليلها الإجماع والرابع عدم لزوم الدم لحديث الدارقطني المفيد لذلك لكنه ضعيف لكن تعددت طرقه فصار حسناً . وأشار بقوله فليحل بعمرة إلى وجوبها كما صرح به في البدائع، وإلى أنه يطوف ويسعى ثم يحلق أو يقصر وإلى أن إحرامه لا ينقلب إحرام عمرة بل يخرج عن إحرام الحج بأفعال العمرة وهو قولهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف ويشهد لهما أن القارن إذا فاته
إحصار بعد الوقوف. قوله (قيل لا يتحلل في مكانه أي ليس له أن يحلق في الحل في الحال بل يؤخر الحلق إلى ما بعد طواف الزيارة قوله قال العتابي وهو الأظهر) قال في النهر كأنه لا مكان حمل الإطلاق في الأصل على هذا القيد اهـ واعترض أو لا بأنه يلزم على هذا أن لا يكون بينهما خلاف فيكون معنى ما في الأصل من أنه حرام أي على النساء فقط ويأباه ترجيح العتابي بأن ما في الجامع أظهر إذ على فرض صحة هذا الحمل لم يبق حاجة للترجيح وثانياً بان قوله في الأصل وهو حرام مطلقاً ظاهر في بقاء الإحرام في حق النساء وغيرهن فالحق أنه قول مقابل اهـ قلت قد يجاب بأن عبارة الأصل وإن كانت ظاهرة في بقاء الإحرام مطلقاً إلا أنها محتملة للتقييد ولما كانت عبارة الجامع صريحة في ذلك كانت أظهر إذ لا شك أن الصريح أظهر من المحتمل قول المصنف (ومن منع بمكة عن الركنين قال الرملي في الفيض للكركي ولو حاضت قبل طواف الزيارة ولم تطهر وأراد الرفقة العود تهجم وتطوف حائضاً وتذبح بدنة ولكن لا نفتي بالتهجم فإن لم تطف تبقى محرمة أبداً إلى أن تطوف وكذا الرجل لو لم يطفه .
باب الفوات
قوله (الثالث لزوم القضاء قال الرملي إن قيل كيف توصف حجة الإسلام بالقضاء ولا وقت لها فالجواب أن المراد بالقضاء القضاء اللغوي لا القضاء الحقيقي وقيل لأنه لما أحرم بها تضيق وقتها
Chapter of Fawat (Missing). His statement: (Whoever misses Hajj by missing the standing at Arafah, then let him exit with Umrah, and upon him is Hajj next year without blood.) This is an explanation of four rulings: First, that missing Hajj only occurs by missing the standing at Arafah with the passing of its time. Second, that if he misses it, it is obligatory upon him to exit from it with the actions of Umrah. Third, the necessity of making it up, whether what he began was the Hajj of Islam, a vow, or voluntary. There is no disagreement among the Ummah on these three, so their evidence is ijma‘. Fourth, the non-necessity of blood, due to the hadith of al-Daraqutni which indicates that, but it is weak, yet its multiple chains make it hasan. And he indicated by his statement 'let him exit with Umrah' its obligation, as stated in al-Bada’i‘, and that he performs Tawaf and Sa‘y, then shaves or trims, and that his ihram does not transform into ihram of Umrah, rather he exits from the ihram of Hajj by the actions of Umrah. This is the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf. And their proof is that the qarin when he misses...
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This section from Bab al-Fawat of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the rulings for one who misses Hajj (fawat). The matn states: 'Whoever misses Hajj by missing the standing at Arafah, let him exit with Umrah, and upon him is Hajj next year without blood.' The commentator enumerates four rulings: (1) The only way to miss Hajj is by missing Wuquf at Arafah after its time expires. (2) Upon missing, one must perform the actions of Umrah (Tawaf, Sa‘y, shaving/trimming) to exit ihram. (3) He must make up the Hajj (qada') in the following year, whether the missed Hajj was the obligatory Hajj of Islam, a vowed Hajj, or a voluntary one. There is consensus (ijma‘) on these three. (4) No blood sacrifice (dam) is required for this fawat, based on a hadith from al-Daraqutni, which is weak but strengthened by multiple chains. The commentary also clarifies that the ihram of Hajj does not transform into an ihram of Umrah; rather, the person exits the Hajj ihram by performing Umrah actions. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf held a different view. The passage then includes additional discussions about tahallul after standing and prevention at Makkah, but the core lesson focuses on the rulings of fawat. The author also addresses an objection about qada' of Hajj al-Islam, explaining that qada' here is linguistic (making up) not technical (repeating an obligation that had a specific time).

Key Terms
الفوات (al-fawat): Missing Hajj by failing to perform the standing at Arafah before its time expires.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up the missed Hajj the following year; here it is linguistic qada', not technical qada' of a timed obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence for the fourth ruling (no blood sacrifice) concerning fawat?
A) Ijma' of the Companions
B) A hadith from al-Daraqutni
C) Qiyas on ihsar
D) The Quranic verse 2:196

Key Takeaway
Fawat (missing Hajj) is defined only by missing Wuquf at Arafah; the pilgrim must exit with Umrah actions, repeat Hajj next year, and owes no blood sacrifice – this is the Hanafi ruling supported by ijma‘ (on the first three) and a hadith (on the fourth).

Hajj — Pilgrimage

Missing Hajj: Obligation of Umrah and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on the ruling for one who misses Hajj and the obligation to perform Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on missing Hajj and distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding the transformation of ihram.

Core Idea
When Hajj is missed, the ihram does not transform—you perform Umrah to exit, not to switch.

Imagine you are on the plain of Arafat, the sun sets, and you missed the standing. What now? The Hanafi school has a clear ruling: you must exit your ihram by performing the actions of Umrah, then repeat Hajj next year without a blood sacrifice. But a subtle internal dispute arises: does your ihram transform into an Umrah ihram, or do you simply exit the original Hajj ihram? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks both the consensus rulings and the intra-Hanafi debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four rulings
Extract the four rulings from the matn: (1) missing Hajj is defined by missing the standing at Arafat, (2) the person must exit ihram by performing Umrah actions, (3) qada of Hajj is wajib for the next year, (4) no blood sacrifice is required.
These four constitute the core position.
2
Locate the evidence
For the first three rulings, the evidence is ijma (consensus). For the fourth, a hadith from al-Daraqutni, weak individually but strengthened by multiple chains to hasan status.
Knowing the evidence levels helps assess the strength of each ruling.
3
Recognize the dispute
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf differs from the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). He holds that the ihram transforms into an Umrah ihram, while the two maintain it does not – the person simply exits the Hajj ihram by performing Umrah actions.
This is an intra-Hanafi legitimate ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the proofs
Examine the proofs for each side: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad use the qarin case (cannot combine two Umrahs) and the mutamatti' scenario; Abu Yusuf uses the case of remaining in ihram until next year where the original ihram does not suffice for Hajj.
The text provides detailed analogical arguments.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn). The author of al-Nihaya and later scholars (like al-Marghinani) prefer this view. The practical consequence (thamarah) appears in the case of intending a second Hajj after fawt.
This step shows how a dispute is resolved within the school.

Whoever misses Hajj due to missing the standing at Arafat should perform Umrah and must perform Hajj the following year without a blood sacrifice. Yusuf: The correct view is the detailed one mentioned earlier, as in al-Nihaya. This is a reference to rejecting what is in al-Muhit, which considers the detail in al-Mukhtasar as a rare narration (nawadir) and that the apparent ruling (zahir al-riwaya) is that being prevented (ihsar) in Makkah according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is not ihsar because it is rare and not considered. Chapter of Fawt: His statement: (Whoever misses Hajj due to missing the standing at Arafat should perform Umrah and must perform Hajj the following year without blood) is an explanation of four rulings: First, that missing Hajj only occurs by missing the standing at Arafat with the passing of its time. Second, that when he misses it, he must exit it by the actions of Umrah. Third, the obligation of making it up (qada) whether what he began was the obligatory Hajj of Islam, a vow, or a voluntary one, and there is no disagreement among the Ummah on these three, so their evidence is consensus (ijma). Fourth: the non-obligation of blood due to the hadith of al-Daraqutni that indicates that, but it is weak, but its multiple chains have made it good (hasan). And his statement 'should perform Umrah' indicates its obligation as stated in al-Bada'i, and that he performs tawaf and sa'y, then shaves or shortens, and that his ihram does not transform into an ihram of Umrah, rather he exits the ihram of Hajj by the actions of Umrah, which is the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf. And supporting them is that if a qarin misses [Hajj after] ihsar after standing. His statement: (It is said he should not shave in his place, meaning he should not shave in the state of halal immediately, but should delay shaving until after tawaf al-ziyarah; his statement: al-Atabi said it is more apparent). He said in al-Nahr: as if it is possible to restrict the absolute in the original text by this condition. It was objected firstly that this would require that there be no difference between them, so the meaning of what is in the original that he is haram means only women, but the tarjih of al-Atabi refutes this because what is in al-Jami' is more apparent; if this interpretation were correct, there would be no need for tarjih. Secondly, his statement in the original that he is absolutely haram is apparent in the continuation of ihram regarding women and others, so the truth is that it is a contrasting position. I say: it can be answered that although the wording of the original is apparent in the continuation of ihram absolutely, it is possible to restrict it, and since the wording of al-Jami' is explicit (sarih) in that, it is more apparent, as there is no doubt that the explicit is more apparent than the probable. The author's statement: (And whoever is prevented in Makkah from the two pillars: al-Ramli said in al-Fayd on behalf of al-Kurki: If a woman menstruates before tawaf al-ziyarah and does not become pure, and her companions want to return, she forcibly performs tawaf while menstruating and sacrifices a camel, but we do not give fatwa of forcing; if she does not perform tawaf, she remains in ihram forever until she performs tawaf; similarly a man if he does not perform tawaf. Chapter of Fawt: His statement (Third: the necessity of qada: al-Ramli said: If it is said how can Hajj of Islam be described as qada when it has no time, the answer is that what is meant by qada is linguistic qada, not real qada; and it is said because when he began it, its time became restricted. There is no missing of Umrah; it is tawaf and sa'y, and it is valid throughout the year and disliked on the Day of Arafah and the Day of Sacrifice. And the Hajj is performed, its Umrah because it cannot be missed; then he performs another Umrah for the missing of Hajj, then shaves, and there is no [blood] upon him because it is for combining two rites and it did not occur. If his ihram had transformed into Umrah, he would have combined ihrams of two Umrahs and performing them at one time, which is not permissible. And supporting them is that if he remained in ihram until the months of Hajj of the next year entered, and then he exited by the actions of Umrah, then performed Hajj in that same year, he would not be a mutamatti'; if his ihram had transformed into Umrah, he would be a mutamatti' like one who begins Umrah in Ramadan and performs tawaf for it in Shawwal. This is in al-Mabsut. And supporting Abu Yusuf is that if the one who missed Hajj remains in ihram until he performs Hajj with the people the next year with that same ihram, it would not suffice for his Hajj; if the basis of his ihram remained, it would have sufficed. Al-Mabsut answered it that although the basis remains, he is required to exit by the actions of Umrah, and this requirement is not voided by the change of the year; his ihram was contracted for the performance of Hajj in the first year; if the performance of Hajj with it in the second year were valid, the obligation of that contract would change by his action, and he does not have the power to change the obligation of the contract of ihram. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that the benefit of the difference appears when he misses Hajj and then intends another Hajj other than the first: it is valid and the other is rejected according to Abu Hanifa; according to Muhammad, it is not valid; and according to Abu Yusuf, he continues with the other because according to him the first ihram has transformed into Umrah, so he is in ihram for Umrah and he has added Hajj to it; and according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), since his ihram remains, when he intends another Hajj, he rejects it so as not to combine two ihrams of Hajj, and upon him is blood, Umrah, and two Hajjs next year. If by the second he intends to make up the missed one, then it is the same and he must make it up because he remains in the ihram of Hajj; when he intends qada, he intends the existing ihram, so his intention is not valid and he does not become in ihram by another ihram. And 'fawt al-Hajj' is absolute, so it includes both the corrupt and the valid Hajj. So if one begins Hajj then corrupts it by intercourse before standing, then misses Hajj, he must pay blood for intercourse and exit by Umrah because the corrupt is considered like the valid. Similarly, if it is contracted as corrupt, like when one begins Hajj while in a state of janabah, it is attached to the valid. And the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'Because the ihram after it has been validly contracted cannot be exited except by performing one of the two rites' is interpreted as applying to the necessary (necessary) to exclude the non-necessary, like the slave and the wife when they begin without permission, not what is opposite of valid, which is the corrupt. And to exclude by it the case where one enters Hajj upon Umrah or upon Umrah, for it is not necessary, so rejection is obligatory; and the muhsar is not included because his ihram is necessary even though he exits it without actions, because it is an impediment, not by way of original constitution.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the chapter of fawt (missing) of Hajj. The author explains the four rulings of the matn: (1) Fawt only occurs by missing the standing at Arafat, (2) the one who misses must exit ihram by performing the actions of Umrah (tawaf, sa'y, halq or taqsir), (3) he must make up the Hajj in a future year, and (4) no blood sacrifice is required. The evidence for the first three is ijma' (consensus), and for the fourth, the hadith of al-Daraqutni which, though weak individually, is strengthened by multiple chains. The commentary then addresses a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn), the ihram does NOT transform into an ihram of Umrah; rather, one exits the original Hajj ihram by performing Umrah actions. Abu Yusuf holds that the ihram transforms into an Umrah ihram. The author provides supporting evidence for both positions from the rulings of the qarin (one combining Hajj and Umrah) and the case of one who remains in ihram until the next year. The dispute is further illustrated by the scenario of intending another Hajj after fawt: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the second is rejected, Abu Yusuf says it is accepted. The text also discusses corrupt Hajj, the ruling of a woman who menstruates before tawaf al-ziyarah, and a nuanced discussion about the meaning of 'qada' for Hajjat al-Islam. The commentary clarifies that the mushrik's ihram is not lifted by force but by a valid excuse. The authority weight of the main ruling is Zahir al-Riwayah as per the matn, with the detailed positions being from al-Nihaya and al-Mabsut.

Key Terms
الفوات (al-fawat): The state of missing the Hajj by failing to perform the standing at Arafat within its time.
الإحلال بالعمرة (al-ihlal bil-umrah): The process of exiting ihram by performing the actions of Umrah (tawaf, sa'y, halq or taqsir) when Hajj is missed.
القضاء (al-qada): The obligation to perform the missed Hajj in a subsequent year; here interpreted linguistically, not as a true qada since Hajjat al-Islam has no specific time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what happens to the ihram of a person who misses the standing at Arafat?
A) It transforms into an ihram for Umrah.
B) It remains as an ihram for Hajj and the person must remain in it until the next year.
C) It does not transform; the person exits the Hajj ihram by performing the actions of Umrah.
D) It becomes invalid and the person must repeat the entire Hajj from the beginning.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi view: missing Hajj requires exiting by Umrah (without transformation) and repeating Hajj next year without blood.

Definition and Actions of Umrah and the Basis for Qada
Definition and ruling on Umrah's actions and qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of Umrah, identify its actions (tawaf and sa'y), explain why it has no fawt, and summarise the Hanafi response to the objection on qada.

Core Idea
Umrah has no fixed time and its pillar is tawaf; the obligation of qada is proven by general evidence.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the nature of Umrah and its actions. It first establishes that Umrah is not time-bound, so it cannot be 'missed' like Hajj. Then it explains that the acts of Umrah are tawaf and sa'y, with tawaf being its essential pillar. Finally, it addresses an objection: if qada of actions after fawt were obligatory, why was it not transmitted? The Hanafi response is that the general evidence of obligation suffices, and the lack of transmission does not negate the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its temporal scope
Recognise that Umrah is distinguished from Hajj by having no fixed time. The text states 'لا فوت لعمرة' because of consensus on its lack of a specified time.
Fawt refers to missing the prescribed time of an act.
2
List the actions of Umrah
Extract from the text that the actions (أفعال) of Umrah are tawaf (seven circuits) and sa'y (between Safa and Marwa).
The text does not specify the classification of sa'y (wajib or rukn) here.
3
Distinguish the pillar (rukn) from other actions
Note the commentator's clarification that the author did not intend a complete definition; the pillar (rukn) of Umrah is tawaf, just as prayer has its own pillars.
This is a key Hanafi point: tawaf is the indispensible element.
4
Understand the objection regarding qada
The objection is: if the Prophet (peace be upon him) had ordered qada of actions after fawt, why did the Sahaba not transmit it?
The objection uses lack of transmission as a sign that the order did not occur.
5
Analyse the Hanafi response
The response is that the general evidence (dalil) of obligation is established; the absence of specific transmission does not refute it. The ruling of qada is derived from that general proof.
This illustrates the Hanafi method of relying on established usul even when specific historical accounts are absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا فوت لعمرة) لعدم توقيتها بالإجماع قوله : (وهي طواف وسعي) أي أفعال العمرة طواف بالبيت سبعة أشواط وسعي بين الصفا والمروة. وليس مراده بيان ماهيتها لأن ركنها الطواف كما قالوا في الصلاة يفسدها ثم يفعلها في الوقت فالحج أولى بذلك تأمل قوله (نعم هو) أي عدم نقل الأمر بالقضاء مما يؤنس به في عدم وقوع الأمر بحسب الظاهر وإلا لنقل لا أنه يصلح دليلاً على وقوله «لكن ذلك الخ جواب عن الاستئناس المذكور، وحاصله أن دليل الوجوب مطلقاً ثابت فيجب الحكم بعلمهم به وقضائها كما هو مقتضى ذلك الدليل من غير تعيين من أين علموا به ليخرج عدمه .
His statement: (There is no fawt for Umrah) because it has no fixed time by consensus. His statement: (It is tawaf and sa'y) meaning the actions of Umrah are tawaf around the House seven circuits and sa'y between Safa and Marwa. And he does not intend to define its essence because its pillar is tawaf as they said about prayer that it invalidates it then he does it in its time, so Hajj is more deserving of that; reflect. His statement: (Yes it is) meaning that the lack of transmission of the matter concerning qada gives comfort that the matter did not occur apparently, otherwise it would have been transmitted; but it does not serve as evidence. And his statement: 'But that...' is a response to the aforementioned seeking comfort, and its summary is that the evidence of obligation is absolute and established, so judgment must be by their knowledge of it and its qada as required by that evidence, without specifying from where they knew it, to exclude its absence.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the chapter of fawt. First, the author explains why Umrah is not subject to fawt: because it has no fixed time, by consensus. All months and seasons are suitable for Umrah, though some times are disliked (like the Day of Arafah and the Day of Sacrifice). Second, he clarifies the actions of Umrah: tawaf (seven circuits) and sa'y (between Safa and Marwa). He notes that the author of the matn did not intend to give a complete definition of Umrah; its essential pillar (rukn) is the tawaf, similar to how prayer has essential elements. The final part addresses an objection: if the obligation of shaving and completing the actions were universally known, why wasn't it transmitted? The answer is that the evidence for the obligation is general and established; the lack of specific transmission does not disprove the ruling. The Hanafi position relies on the established proof (dalil) of obligation, even if the historical record does not detail every incident.

Key Terms
العمرة (al-umrah): A minor pilgrimage consisting of tawaf and sa'y, which can be performed at any time of the year.
الطواف (al-tawaf): Circumambulation around the Kaaba seven times, a pillar of Umrah and Hajj.
السعي (al-sa'y): Walking between the hills of Safa and Marwa seven times, an action of Umrah and Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the objection that lack of transmission about qada invalidates the obligation?
A) Because the Sahaba deliberately hid the ruling.
B) Because qada is proven by ijtihad, not text.
C) Because the general evidence of obligation is established and the absence of specific transmission does not refute it.
D) Because Umrah is not obligatory, so qada is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Umrah has no fixed time, its pillar is tawaf, and the obligation of qada is proven by the general evidence of obligation, not by specific transmission.

Definition and Rulings of 'Umrah: No Fawt, Validity Throughout Year, Disliked Days
Defines 'Umrah, states its validity and disliked times, and explains its acts and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of 'Umrah, identify its obligatory and conditional elements, and state the ruling regarding its timing and disliked days.

Core Idea
'Umrah is not time-bound like Hajj; it is valid all year except five specific days.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq offers a concise definition of 'Umrah and clarifies its key rulings: no fawt (missing the time) because it is not time-bound, validity throughout the year, and dislike on five days. It also distinguishes the acts that constitute 'Umrah into pillars, obligations, and conditions. Understanding this lays the foundation for performing 'Umrah correctly according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State that 'Umrah is not subject to fawt because it is not time-bound, as proven by consensus (ijma).
The phrase 'la fawt' is a ruling of non-invalidity due to time.
2
Extract the legal definition
Note the definition: 'Umrah consists of tawaf (seven circuits around the Ka'bah) and sa'y (walking between Safa and Marwah).
The linguistic meaning is 'visitation'; the legal meaning adds specific rituals.
3
Classify the components by legal weight
Distinguish between pillars (arkan), obligations (wajibat), and conditions (shurut). Tawaf is a rukn, sa'y and halq are wajib, and ihram is a shart.
Hanafi fiqh: omission of rukn invalidates; omission of wajib requires compensation.
4
Note the disliked days
Memorize the five days: Day of 'Arafah, Day of Nahr (10th Dhul Hijjah), and the three Days of Tashriq (11th-13th Dhul Hijjah).
Dislike is makruh tanzihi; the 'Umrah is still valid if performed.
5
Observe the linguistic and legal link
Understand that linguistically 'Umrah means visitation, which informs its legal concept of visiting the Ka'bah with specific rites.
The passage explicitly gives the linguistic definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا فوت لعمرة وهي طواف وسعي وتصح في السنة وتكره يوم عرفة ويوم النحر وأيام التشريق وهي مؤكدة. قوله : (ولا فوت لعمرة) لعدم توقيتها بالإجماع قوله : (وهي طواف وسعي) أي أفعال العمرة طواف بالبيت سبعة أشواط وسعي بين الصفا والمروة. وأما السعي فواجب وإنما لم يصرح بوجوبه فيها للعلم به من الحج لأن السعي فيه واجب ففي العمرة أولى. ولم يذكر الإحرام لأنه شرط في النسكين حجاً كان أو عمرة، ولم يذكر الحلق لأنه محل مخرج منها وهو من واجباتها كما في فتاوى قاضيخان. وهي في اللغة بمعنى الزيارة، يقال اعتمر فلان فلاناً إذا زاره.
And there is no missing (fawt) for 'Umrah, and it consists of circumambulation (tawaf) and running (sa'y), and it is valid throughout the year, and it is disliked on the Day of 'Arafah, the Day of Nahr (Sacrifice), and the Days of Tashriq, and it is confirmed (mu'akkadah). He said: (And there is no fawt for 'Umrah) because it is not time-bound by consensus. He said: (And it is tawaf and sa'y) meaning the acts of 'Umrah are: circumambulation of the House (Ka'bah) seven circuits, and running between Safa and Marwah. As for sa'y, it is obligatory; and he did not mention its obligation explicitly in it because it is known from Hajj, since sa'y is obligatory in Hajj, so in 'Umrah it is more appropriate. And he did not mention ihram because it is a condition in both rituals, whether Hajj or 'Umrah; and he did not mention shaving (halq) because it is a means of exiting from it, and it is among the obligatory acts of 'Umrah, as in Fatawa Qadikhan. And in language, 'Umrah means visitation; one says 'A'tamara fulan fulanan' if he visited him.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a definition-rich ruling. It opens by stating that 'Umrah is not subject to fawt (missing the time) because it is not time-bound, a matter established by consensus (ijma). It defines 'Umrah legally as consisting of two main actions: tawaf (seven circuits around the Ka'bah) and sa'y (walking between Safa and Marwah). The text further clarifies that sa'y is obligatory (wajib), not merely recommended, deriving this from the status of sa'y in Hajj, which is also obligatory. The author notes that the original matn does not explicitly mention the obligation of sa'y because it is understood from Hajj. Additionally, the text explains that ihram is a condition (shart) for both Hajj and 'Umrah, not a pillar, and that shaving/trimming hair (halq) is among the obligatory acts (wajibat) of 'Umrah. Finally, the linguistic meaning of 'Umrah is given as 'visitation.' The ruling that 'Umrah is valid throughout the year but disliked on five specific days—Day of 'Arafah, Day of Nahr, and the three days of Tashriq—is stated. The word 'mu'akkadah' indicates that this dislike is confirmed, implying it is a strong recommendation to avoid these days. This passage is a typical example of Hanafi legal exposition: a concise matn followed by a sharh that supplies proofs (consensus), definitions, and hierarchical rulings (wajib vs. rukn vs. shart).

Key Terms
عمرة ('Umrah): Legally: performance of tawaf around the Ka'bah and sa'y between Safa and Marwah with ihram; linguistically: visitation.
طواف (Tawaf): Circumambulation of the Ka'bah seven times, a pillar (rukn) of both Hajj and 'Umrah.
سعي (Sa'y): Walking between the hills of Safa and Marwah seven times, an obligatory act (wajib) in 'Umrah and Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on which day is it disliked to perform 'Umrah?
A) Day of 'Arafah
B) First day of Ramadan
C) 15th of Sha'ban
D) 10th of Muharram

Key Takeaway
'Umrah is a confirmed sunnah consisting of tawaf and sa'y with ihram; it is valid throughout the year except on five specified days, and its lack of time boundness is proven by consensus.

The Ruling on 'Umrah: Sunnah Mu'akkadah or Wajib?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of 'Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on 'Umrah and explain why the majority hold it as sunnah mu'akkadah despite the wajib opinion.

Core Idea
'Umrah is sunnah mu'akkadah, not wajib, because the Prophet explicitly rejected obligation.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: is 'Umrah wajib or sunnah? The commentator clarifies that the correct position is sunnah mu'akkadah, supported by the hadith of Jabir where the Prophet said 'Umrah is not obligatory but performing it is better. The verse 'complete the Hajj and 'Umrah for Allah' is understood to refer to completion after starting, not initial obligation. Despite some Hanafi scholars holding it as wajib, the majority and the relied-upon position is sunnah mu'akkadah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions within the Hanafi school
Note that the matn says 'it is sunnah' but some scholars like those of al-Jawharah and al-Bada'i say it is wajib. The commentator states the correct position is sunnah mu'akkadah.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
2
Locate the primary dalil for the sunnah position
The hadith of Jabir: the Prophet was asked if 'Umrah is obligatory; he said 'no' and that performing it is better. This hadith is the key proof that it is not wajib.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), but in Hanafi usul, ahad hadith can establish obligation if the context indicates, but here it negates obligation.
3
Interpret the Quranic verse (2:196)
The verse 'complete the Hajj and 'Umrah for Allah' is interpreted to mean completion after one has started (shuru'), which is binding. It does not prove initial obligation before starting.
The commentator emphasizes that the dispute is about obligation before starting; after starting, it becomes binding.
4
Understand the tarjih (preference) mechanism
The commentator prefers the view of Imam Muhammad from al-Mukhtasar, who calls 'Umrah nafl, and uses the hadith of Jabir to support this. The wajib view is considered weak.
The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah, as Imam Muhammad's statement is in the core books.
5
Derive the practical ruling
'Umrah is sunnah mu'akkadah once in a lifetime; performing it in Ramadan is most virtuous. This does not affect the status of Hajj or qiran.
The passage also mentions that qiran (combining Hajj and 'Umrah) is better for Hajj, but that is a separate issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهي سنة) أي العمرة سنة مؤكدة وهو الصحيح في المذهب، وقيل بوجوبها وصححه في الجوهرة واختاره في البدائع وقال : إنه مذهب أصحابنا. ومنهم من أطلق اسم السنة وهذا لا ينافي الوجوب ا هـ . والظاهر من الرواية ما في المختصر فإن محمداً نص في كتاب الحجر أن العمرة تطوع وليس بينهما كبير فرق كما قدمناه مراراً. واستدل لها في غاية البيان بما رواه الترمذي وصححه عن جابر أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن العمرة أواجبة هي؟ قال: لا، وأن تعتمروا هو أفضل وأما قوله تعالى وأتموا الحج والعمرة الله ﴾ [البقرة: ١٩٦] فالإتمام بعد الشروع ولا كلام لنافيه لأن الشروع ملزم وكلامنا فيما قبل الشروع. والمراد أنها سنة في العمرة مرة واحدة فمن أتى بها مرة فقد أقام السنة غير مقيد بوقت غير ما ثبت النهي عنها فيه إلا أنها في رمضان أفضل هذا إذا أفردها فلا ينافيه أن القرآن أفضل لأن ذلك أمر يرجع إلى الحج لا العمرة. فالحاصل أن من أراد الإتيان بالعمرة على وجه أفضل فيها ففي رمضان أو الحج على وجه أفضل فبأن يقرن معه عمرة، ثم اعلم أن للعمرة معنى لغوياً
His statement: (And it is sunnah) meaning 'Umrah is a strongly recommended sunnah, and this is the correct position in the madhhab. It has been said that it is obligatory, and this was corrected in al-Jawharah and chosen in al-Bada'i, stating that it is the madhhab of our companions. Some of them used the term sunnah, but this does not negate obligation. The apparent from the narration is what is in al-Mukhtasar: Muhammad explicitly stated in Kitab al-Hajr that 'Umrah is nafl, and there is no great difference between them. Evidence is the hadith of Jabir: the Prophet was asked whether 'Umrah is obligatory; he said no, and performing it is better. As for the verse 'complete the Hajj and 'Umrah for Allah', that refers to completion after starting, and there is no dispute about that because starting makes it binding; our discussion is before starting. The meaning is that 'Umrah is sunnah once, so whoever performs it once has established the sunnah, not restricted to a time other than when prohibition is established. However, in Ramadan it is better. This is when performed separately, and it does not contradict that qiran is better because that relates to Hajj, not 'Umrah. So the conclusion: whoever wants to perform 'Umrah in the best way should do it in Ramadan; if they want Hajj in the best way, they should combine it with 'Umrah. Then know that 'Umrah has a linguistic meaning...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether 'Umrah is wajib or sunnah mu'akkadah. The matn states it is sunnah, which the commentator explains as sunnah mu'akkadah — the correct position in the madhhab. However, some Hanafi scholars like the author of al-Jawharah and al-Bada'i hold it is wajib, claiming that is the madhhab of the early companions. The commentator prefers the view of Imam Muhammad in al-Mukhtasar that 'Umrah is nafl (supererogatory). The key proof is the authentic hadith of Jabir: the Prophet explicitly said 'Umrah is not obligatory, and performing it is better. The verse 'complete the Hajj and 'Umrah for Allah' is interpreted to mean completion after one has started, which is obligatory because starting makes it binding, but it does not prove obligation before starting. The passage concludes that 'Umrah is a sunnah to be performed once in a lifetime, and the best time is Ramadan, while the best form of Hajj is qiran. It also notes that the linguistic meaning of 'Umrah is 'visit'.

Key Terms
سنة مؤكدة (Sunnah Mu’akkadah): A strongly recommended act; failure to perform it is blameworthy but not sinful, and it is not wajib.
تطوع (Tatawwu‘): Supererogatory act of worship; here, Imam Muhammad calls 'Umrah nafl, meaning it is not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
'Umrah is sunnah mu'akkadah in the Hanafi school; the minority wajib opinion is outweighed by the explicit hadith and the correct interpretation of the Quran.

Dedicating Reward of Good Deeds (Ihda' al-Thawab)
Opening of the chapter on performing Hajj for another person, discussing the general ruling on dedicating reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dedicating reward of good deeds to others, cite the primary evidence from Quran and Sunnah, and explain the Hanafi resolution of the apparent conflict with verse 39 of Surah al-Najm.

Core Idea
You can gift the reward of your worship to others; the Quran and Sunnah confirm it, and the contrary texts are interpreted through the gift analogy.

This lesson covers the important principle of إهداء الثواب (dedicating reward) in Hanafi fiqh. We will explore the evidence from Quran and Sunnah that establishes permissibility, and then see how the school resolves apparent contradictions from other texts. Understanding this ruling is essential for knowing the scope of one's act of worship and its benefit to others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the good deeds covered
The text lists prayer, fasting, charity, Quran recitation, dhikr, tawaf, Hajj, Umrah, and others. Note that the Hanafi school includes all acts of worship.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive.
2
State the Hanafi ruling on dedicating reward
It is permissible (ja'iz) and the reward reaches the intended person, whether living or dead.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Verse 17:24 (prayer for parents) and 40:7-9 (angels seeking forgiveness) show that supplication benefits others, implying reward transfer.
The angels' istighfar is a form of deed reward transfer.
4
Examine the Sunnah evidence
The Prophet sacrificed one ram for his whole Ummah (Sahihayn) and said 'Recite Ya Sin over your dead' (Abu Dawud). These show that rewards can be dedicated and reach the deceased.
The sacrifice hadith is especially strong.
5
Resolve the apparent conflict with al-Najm 39
The verse 'man has nothing but his effort' is not taken literally; Ibn al-Humam clarifies that it is restricted by the worker's gift. Without the gift, one cannot claim another's effort. The hadith 'no one acts for another' concerns fulfilling obligations, not rewarding.
This interpretation upholds both texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحج عن الغير لما كان الحج عن الغير كالتبع أخره، والأصل فيه أن الإنسان له أن يجعل ثواب عمله لغيره صلاة أو صوماً أو صدقة أو قراءة قرآن أو ذكراً أو طوافاً أو حجاً أو عمرة أو غير ذلك عند أصحابنا للكتاب والسنة. أما الكتاب فلقوله تعالى وقل رب ارحمهما كما ربياني صغيرا [الإسراء: ٢٤] وإخباره تعالى عن ملائكته بقوله ويستغفرون للذين آمنوا [غافر : ] وساق عبارتهم بقوله تعالى ربنا وسعت كل شيء رحمة وعلما فاغفر للذين تابوا واتبعوا سبيلك إلى قوله وقهم السيئات [غافر: ۹] وأما السنة فأحاديث كثيرة منها ما في الصحيحين حين ضحى بالكبشين فجعل أحدهما عن أمته وهو مشهور تجوز الزيادة به على الكتاب. ومنها ما رواه أبو داود «اقرؤوا على موتاكم سورة يس (۱). وحينئذ فتعين. أن لا يكون قوله تعالى وأن ليس للإنسان إلا ما سعى [النجم: ۳۹] عى ظاهره وفيه تأويلات أقربها ما اختاره المحقق ابن الهمام أنها مقيدة بما يهبه العامل يعني ليس للإنسان من سعي غيره نصيب إلا إذا وهبه له فحينئذ يكون له، وأما قوله عليه السلام «لا أحد عن ولا يصلي أحد عن أحد» فهو في حق الخروج عن العهدة لا في حق الثواب فإن من صام أو صلى أو تصدق وجعل ثوابه لغيره من الأموات والأحياء جاز ويصل ثوابها إليهم عند أهل
Chapter of Hajj on Behalf of Another. Since Hajj on behalf of another is like a subsidiary, he delayed it. The principle regarding it is that a person can assign the reward of his deed to another: prayer, fasting, charity, Quran recitation, dhikr, tawaf, Hajj, 'Umrah, or other than that, according to our companions (as per the Book and Sunnah). As for the Book, His statement (Exalted): 'And say, My Lord, have mercy upon them as they brought me up [when I was] small' [al-Isra: 24], and His informing about His angels with His statement: 'And they ask forgiveness for those who have believed' [Ghafir], and continued their statement with His words: 'Our Lord, You have encompassed all things in mercy and knowledge, so forgive those who have repented and followed Your way' up to 'and protect them from the evil deeds' [Ghafir: 9]. As for the Sunnah, there are many hadith, among them what is in the Sahihayn when he sacrificed two rams, making one for his Ummah, which is well known, and the increase over the Book is permissible. Among them is what Abu Dawud narrated: 'Recite Surah Ya Sin over your dead.' So it is established that His statement 'And that man has nothing but what he strives for' [al-Najm: 39] is not literal. There are interpretations, the closest being what al-Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam chose: it is restricted by what the worker gives as a gift, meaning man has no share of another's striving unless he gifts it to him, then it becomes his. As for his statement (peace be upon him): 'No one [acts] for [another], and no one prays for another,' it concerns relieving liability, not regarding reward, for whoever fasts, prays, or gives charity and assigns its reward to another, whether dead or alive, it is permissible and its reward reaches them according to the [authorities]
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Hajj on behalf of others. It first establishes the general principle: a person may dedicate the reward of any good deed (including prayer, fasting, charity, Quran recitation, dhikr, tawaf, Hajj, 'Umrah) to another person, whether alive or dead, according to the Hanafi school. Evidence from the Quran includes verses about praying for parents and the angels asking forgiveness for believers. Evidence from the Sunnah includes the Prophet sacrificing one ram for his entire Ummah and the hadith about reciting Surah Ya Sin for the dead. The apparently contradictory verse 'That man has nothing but what he strives for' is interpreted not literally; rather, it is understood that one cannot claim the reward of another's deed unless that person gifts it. Similarly, the hadith 'No one acts for another' refers to fulfilling obligations, not to dedicating reward. Thus, dedicating reward is valid and reaches the intended person.

Key Terms
إهداء الثواب (ihda' al-thawab): The act of dedicating the reward of a good deed to another person, living or dead.
الحج عن الغير (al-hajj 'an al-ghayr): Performing Hajj on behalf of another person who is unable to do it themselves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on dedicating the reward of good deeds to others?
A) Not permissible, as per 'man has nothing but his effort'
B) Permissible, supported by Quran and Sunnah
C) Only for prayer, not Hajj
D) Only for the dead, not the living

Key Takeaway
Dedicating reward of good deeds to others, living or dead, is permissible in the Hanafi school, supported by Quran and Sunnah, with the apparent contradicting texts interpreted through the gift analogy.
