Gifting Reward of Deeds: Intention Required?
Discussion on gifting reward of deeds with two positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on gifting reward, identify the evidence for the requirement of intention, and apply the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Reward follows intention — without prior intention, gifting reward after the act is invalid according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Many Muslims wish to gift the reward of their good deeds to loved ones, living or deceased. Is this valid without specifying intention at the time? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two Hanafi positions, with the later scholars requiring intention, supported by the practice of the Companions. Understanding this ikhtilaf helps clarify a common practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question: can the reward (thawāb) of an act be transferred to another without the doer having intended that at the time of the act?
The author presents two positions, both attributed to Hanafi scholars.
2
State the first position (later scholars)
The later scholars (al-Qadi and his followers) require that the doer intend the reward for another at the time of the act or before. Otherwise, the reward remains for the doer and cannot be transferred.
This is considered the more precise (أفقه) view.
3
Examine the dalil
The evidence: the Companions asked the Prophet (ﷺ) about performing acts directly for the deceased (e.g., charity on her behalf, Hajj on her behalf), not about gifting the reward of their own acts. This shows the correct understanding.
Analogy: if you pay your own debt, you cannot later reassign it to someone else.
4
State the second position (early scholars)
Some early Hanafis held that intention is not required. The reward belongs to the doer, who can gift it like property.
This view is also attributed to some early authorities but considered less precise.
5
Identify the tarjih and reason
The author favors the first view because it aligns with the practice of the Salaf and is jurisprudentially sound (أفقه).
The second view permits gifting reward after the act; the first requires intention at the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحد من : عن السنة والجماعة. كذا في البدائع. وبهذا علم أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون المجعول له ميتاً أو حياً، والظاهر أنه لا فرق بين أن ينوي به عند الفعل للغير أو يفعله لنفسه، ثم بعد ذلك يجعل ثوابه لغيره لإطلاق كلامه ولم أر حكم من أخذ شيئاً من الدنيا ليجعل شيئاً من عبادته «الروح» وذكر فيها خلافاً عندهم وقال : هذه المسألة غير منصوصة عن الإمام وأحمد والمتقدمين من أصحابه وإنما اشترط ذلك المتأخرون كالقاضي وأتباعه فقيل : إن نواه حال فعله أو قبله وصل إليه وإلا فلا، لأنه لو لم ينوه وقع الثواب للعامل فلا يقبل انتقاله عنه إلى غيره ولهذا لو أدى ديناً . نفسه ثم أراد بعد الأداء أن يجعله عن غيره لم يكن له ذلك، وكذا لو حج أو صام أو صلى لنفسه، ويؤيد هذا أن الذين سألوا النبي الله عن ذلك لم يسألوه عن ثواب إهداء العمل بعده بل عما يفعلونه عن الميت كما قال سعد أينفعها إن تصدقت عنها ولم يقل إن أهدي لها ثواب ما تصدقت به عن نفسي. وكذا قول المرأة الأخرى « أفأحج عنها وقول الرجل الآخر أفأحج عن أبي» ولا يعرف عن الصحابة أنه قال اللهم اجعل ثواب ما عملته لنفسي أو ثواب عملي المتقدم لفلان، فهذا سر الاشتراط وهو أفقه . ومن لم يشترط ذلك يقول الثواب للعامل فإذا تبرع به وأهداه إلى غيره كان يمنزلة ما يهديه إليه من ماله وعلى الأول لا يصح إهداء الثواب الواجب على العامل، وأما على الثاني فقيل يجوز ويجزئ فاعله وقد نقل عن جماعة أنهم جعلوا ثواب أعمالهم من فرض ونفل للمسلمين وقالوا: نلقى الله تعالى بالفقر والإفلاس المجرد والشريعة لا تمنع من ذلك . اهـ ملخصاً قوله (ولم أرحكم من أخذ شيئاً من الدنيا ليجعل شيئاً من عبادته للمعطى الخ) إن كان المراد من العبادة نحو القواءة والذكر فالمعطى يكون أجرة والمفتى به مذهب المتأخرين من جواز الاستئجار على الطاعات، وبنى عليه العلائي جواز الوصية للقراءة على القبر، وإن كان المراد بها الخضوع والتذلل فعدم الصحة ظاهر. قال في حاشية مسكين قال الإمام اللامشي : العبادة عبارة عن الخضوع والتذلل وحدها فعل لا يراد به إلا تعظيم الله تعالى بأمره بخلاف القربة والطاعة فإن القربة ما يتقرب به إلى الله تعالى ويراد بها تعظيم الله تعالى مع إرادة ما وضع له الفعل كبناء الرباطات والمساجد ونحوها فإنها قربة يراد بها وجه الله تعالى مع إرادة الإحسان بالناس وحصول المنفعة لهم، والطاعة ما يجوز لغير الله تعالى. قال تعالى أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم [النساء : ٥٩] والعبادة ما لا يجوز لغير الله تعالى والطاعة موافقة الأمر اهـ. والظاهر أن المراد الأول وأن الإجارة غير صحيحة لأن المنصوص على جوازه تعليم القرآن كما يأتي في المتن زاد في التنوير تبعاً لصدر الشريعة وغيره تعليم الفقه والإمامة والأذان فهذه المفتى به جواز الإجارة عليها في زماننا، وعللوه بحاجة الناس إليه وظهور التواني في الأوامر الدينية وبأن المعلمين كانت لهم عطيات من بيت المال وزيادة رغبة في إقامة الحسبة وأمور الدين كما بسطه تلميذ المؤلف في منحه وأصل المذهب بطلانها للنهي ذلك، ولأن القربة متى وقعت كانت العامل فلا يجوز له أن يأخذ الأجر على عمل وقع له كما في الصوم والصلاة، وتمامه في المنح. فقد ظهر من هذا أن إجازة ما ذكر لمكان الضرورة وأن ما مر عن العلائي غير ظاهر، بل جواز الوصية مبني على المفتى به من عدم كراهة القراءة على القبور،
From: 'One of them: according to the Sunnah and the community. Thus in al-Bada'i. And by this it is known that there is no difference between the one for whom the reward is designated being dead or alive, and apparently there is no difference between intending it at the time of the act for another or doing it for himself and then afterwards making its reward for another, due to the generality of his statement. And I have not seen the ruling of one who takes something from the world to make something of his worship for the giver, and he mentioned a difference among them and said: This issue is not textually transmitted from the Imam [Abu Hanifa] and Ahmad and the early companions, rather the later scholars like al-Qadi and his followers stipulated that. So it was said: If he intended it at the time of his act or before it reaches him, otherwise not, because if he did not intend it, the reward occurs for the doer and it does not accept transfer from him to another. And for this, if he paid a debt for himself and then after payment wanted to make it for another, he cannot do so. Similarly if he performed Hajj, fasted, or prayed for himself. And this is supported by the fact that those who asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about that did not ask about the reward of gifting the deed afterwards, but about what they do for the deceased, as Sa'd said: 'Will it benefit her if I give charity on her behalf?' and did not say 'I gift her the reward of what I gave in charity for myself.' Similarly the saying of the other woman 'Shall I perform Hajj on her behalf?' and the saying of the other man 'Shall I perform Hajj on behalf of my father?' And it is not known from the Companions that they said 'O Allah, make the reward of what I did for myself or the reward of my previous deed for so-and-so.' This is the secret of the condition and it is more jurisprudential. And whoever did not stipulate that says the reward is for the doer; if he gives it as a gift and gifts it to another, it is like what he gifts to him from his wealth. According to the first view, gifting the obligatory reward on the doer is not valid. According to the second, it is said to be permissible and suffices the doer, and it is transmitted from a group that they made the reward of their deeds, obligatory and supererogatory, for the Muslims and said: We meet Allah Ta'ala with pure poverty and bankruptcy, and the Shariah does not prevent that. End of summary. His saying: 'And I have not seen the ruling of one who takes something from the world to make something of his worship for the giver etc.' If what is meant by worship is like recitation and remembrance, then the giver receives a wage and the fatwa is according to the later school of the permissibility of hiring for acts of obedience, and al-`Ala'i built upon it the permissibility of bequest for recitation at the grave. But if what is meant by it is humility and submission, then incorrectness is apparent. In the Hashiya of Miskin: Imam al-Lamishi said: Worship (ibadah) is an expression of humility and submission, its definition is an act intended only for the glorification of Allah Ta'ala by His command, unlike nearness (qurbah) and obedience (ta`ah), for nearness is what one draws near to Allah Ta'ala with, intending glorification of Allah Ta'ala along with intention of what the act is prescribed for, like building ribats and mosques and the like, for they are nearness intended for the sake of Allah Ta'ala along with intending kindness to people and attainment of benefit for them. And obedience is what is permissible for other than Allah Ta'ala. He said: Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you (4:59). And worship is not permissible for other than Allah Ta'ala. And obedience is compliance with command. End. And apparently the first meaning is intended and that hiring is not valid because what is textually allowed is teaching the Quran as will come in the text. Al-Tanwir added, following Sadr al-Shari`ah and others, teaching fiqh, imamate, and adhan. So the fatwa is on the permissibility of hiring for these in our time, and they justified it by people's need and the appearance of laziness in religious orders, and that teachers had stipends from the treasury, and increased desire in establishing the hisbah and religious affairs, as expanded by the author's student in his Manh. And the original school is its invalidity due to the prohibition of that, and because when a good deed occurs, it belongs to the doer, so it is not permissible for him to take wages for a deed that belongs to him, as in fasting and prayer. Its completion is in al-Manh. So it appears from this that the permission for what was mentioned is due to necessity and that what passed from al-`Ala'i is not apparent, rather the permissibility of bequest is based on the relied-upon fatwa of the absence of dislike for recitation at graves.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses two main issues: gifting reward of acts to others and hiring for worship. On the first issue, the author presents two positions. The first position, favored by later Hanafi scholars like al-Qadi, requires that the doer intend at the time of the act that the reward be for another. If he does not intend that, the reward remains for himself and cannot be transferred later. The evidence includes the practice of the Companions who, when asking the Prophet about benefiting the deceased, asked about performing acts directly for them (like charity or Hajj on their behalf), not about gifting the reward of their own deeds after the fact. The second position, attributed to earlier scholars, permits gifting the reward after the act without prior intention, likening it to gifting one's property. The author finds the first view more precise (أفقه). On the second issue, the author examines the ruling on taking worldly compensation for religious acts. He distinguishes between three concepts: ibadah (worship proper, which is pure humility and submission to Allah), qurbah (acts of nearness like building mosques, which also serve worldly benefits), and ta`ah (obedience, which can be to other than Allah). The original Hanafi position is that hiring for acts of worship is invalid, as the act belongs to the doer and cannot be sold. However, due to necessity and people's negligence, the later fatwa permits hiring for teaching Quran, fiqh, imamate, and adhan. This is a dispensation (rukhsa) not a change in the original ruling. The author also notes that the permissibility of bequest for Quran recitation at graves is based on the fatwa that such recitation is not disliked, not on a general permission of hiring.

Key Terms
إهداء الثواب (ihdā' al-thawāb): Gifting the reward of one's religious act to another person, living or dead.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first position presented in the passage, what is required for the reward of an act to reach another person?
A) The doer must make a gift of the reward after completing the act.
B) The doer must intend at the time of the act that the reward be for the other person.
C) The other person must be alive and aware.
D) The doer must perform the act in the presence of the other person.

Key Takeaway
The reward of an act belongs to the doer by default; to transfer it to another, intention must be present at the time of the act according to the stronger Hanafi view.

Hiring for Worship: Permissibility Due to Necessity
Discussion on hiring for acts of worship with distinction between ibadah, qurbah, ta'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the original Hanafi ruling on hiring for worship, the later fatwa that permits it for specific acts, and distinguish between ibadah, qurbah, and ta'ah as defined in the passage.

Core Idea
Hiring for pure worship is invalid, but necessity allows hiring for teaching Quran, fiqh, imamate, and adhan.

In modern times, mosques hire imams, Quran teachers, and muezzins. Is this permissible? The original Hanafi position held that hiring for acts of worship is invalid, but later scholars permitted it for certain acts due to necessity. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the rationale and the key distinctions between types of religious acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling under discussion
The issue: taking worldly compensation for religious acts (e.g., hiring someone to teach Quran, lead prayer, etc.).
This is a ruling-with-proof passage.
2
State the original Hanafi position
The original school (zahir al-riwayah) held that hiring for acts of worship (ibadah) is invalid because the act belongs to the doer and cannot be sold.
Evidence: prohibition of taking wages for acts of worship, analogy to fasting and prayer.
3
Define the three categories: ibadah, qurbah, ta'ah
The author cites al-Lamishi: Ibadah is pure humility and submission, only for Allah. Qurbah is acts of nearness that may have worldly benefits (building mosques). Ta'ah is obedience, which can be to other than Allah.
This distinction is crucial for the ruling.
4
Explain the later fatwa and its justification
Later Hanafi scholars (al-Tanwir, Sadr al-Shari'ah, etc.) permitted hiring for teaching Quran, fiqh, imamate, and adhan due to people's need, laziness in religious duties, and the precedent of stipends from the treasury.
This is a dispensation (rukhsa), not a change in the original ruling.
5
Note the scope and remaining prohibition
The permissibility is limited to those specific acts. Hiring for pure ibadah (e.g., praying on behalf of another) remains invalid.
The bequest for Quran recitation at graves is tied to the fatwa that recitation is not disliked.

The first position, favored by later Hanafi scholars like al-Qadi, requires that the doer intend at the time of the act that the reward be for another. If he does not intend that, the reward remains for himself and cannot be transferred later. The evidence includes the practice of the Companions who, when asking the Prophet about benefiting the deceased, asked about performing acts directly for them (like charity or Hajj on their behalf), not about gifting the reward of their own deeds after the fact. The second position, attributed to earlier scholars, permits gifting the reward after the act without prior intention, likening it to gifting one's property. The author finds the first view more precise (أفقه). On the second issue, the author examines the ruling on taking worldly compensation for religious acts. He distinguishes between three concepts: ibadah (worship proper, which is pure humility and submission to Allah), qurbah (acts of nearness like building mosques, which also serve worldly benefits), and ta`ah (obedience, which can be to other than Allah). The original Hanafi position is that hiring for acts of worship is invalid, as the act belongs to the doer and cannot be sold. However, due to necessity and people's negligence, the later fatwa permits hiring for teaching Quran, fiqh, imamate, and adhan. This is a dispensation (rukhsa) not a change in the original ruling. The author also notes that the permissibility of bequest for Quran recitation at graves is based on the fatwa that such recitation is not disliked, not on a general permission of hiring.

Key Terms
عبادة (ʻibādah): Act of worship defined as humility and submission, intended solely for glorifying Allah, not permissible for anyone else.
قربة (qurbah): Act of nearness to Allah that may also have worldly benefits (e.g., building mosques).
طاعة (ṭāʻah): Obedience, which can be to other than Allah (e.g., obeying parents or rulers).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi fatwa, which of the following is NOT among the acts for which hiring is permitted?
A) Teaching Quran
B) Leading the prayer (imamate)
C) Performing the Hajj on behalf of another
D) Calling the adhan

Key Takeaway
Hiring for pure worship is invalid in the original Hanafi school, but later scholars permitted it for teaching Quran/fiqh, imamate, and adhan due to necessity.

Classification of Worship and the Rules of Proxy (Niyabah) in the Hanafi School
Classification of worship and rules of proxy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify worship into financial, bodily, and composite types and state the Hanafi ruling on proxy for each type, including the condition of permanent incapacity for Hajj.

Core Idea
Proxy in worship depends on the type of hardship: financial acts are always delegable, bodily acts never, and composite (Hajj) only when permanently incapacitated.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes a fundamental framework for understanding when one person can delegate an act of worship to another. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the act involves financial loss, bodily exertion, or both. We will explore the rational basis (qiyas) and the textual exception (Quran/Sunnah) that permits proxy for Hajj in cases of permanent disability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Recognize that acts of worship are divided into purely financial (e.g., zakat, sadaqat al-fitr, expiations), purely bodily (e.g., prayer, fasting, jihad), and composite (Hajj). Note the examples given for each in the passage.
Financial acts involve parting with wealth; bodily acts involve personal physical effort; composite acts involve both.
2
Understand the principle of hardship
The purpose of obligations is trial (ibtilā') and hardship (mashaqqah). For bodily acts, the hardship is personal and cannot be realized by a deputy. For financial acts, hardship is the loss of wealth, which can be achieved by a deputy. Therefore, proxy is always allowed in financial acts and never in bodily acts.
This is the general analogy (qiyas) derived from the nature of each type.
3
Apply qiyas to the composite (Hajj)
Since Hajj includes bodily hardship, by qiyas proxy should not be allowed at all. However, the passage notes a textual exception: Allah permitted proxy for Hajj for those permanently incapacitated until death, as a mercy. This exception overrides the qiyas.
The condition for this dispensation is permanent incapacity (al-‘ajz al-dā’im) up to the time of death.
4
Note the condition regarding payment
The passage also clarifies that the payment to the proxy must be a gift (silah), not a wage (ujrah). If structured as a wage, it becomes invalid hiring for obedience (ujrah ‘alā al-ṭā‘ah). The same applies to bequests for recitation (tahlīlah or khatm).
The recipient must be specified, and the transaction must not be a contract of hire.

Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling on proxy (niyābah) in different types of worship. It begins by referring to the matn's statement about the composite type (Hajj) being sufficient only in case of incapacity, with the condition of permanent incapacity until death. The commentary then cites the matn's ruling on the three types: financial worship allows proxy in all cases (ability or inability); bodily worship never allows proxy; composite worship (like Hajj) allows proxy only in case of permanent incapacity. The author provides the rationale: bodily worship involves personal hardship which cannot be realized through a deputy; financial worship involves parting with wealth which can be done by a deputy; Hajj includes both, but by qiyas it would not allow proxy, yet Allah permitted it as a mercy when one is permanently unable, by sending expenses to a proxy. The passage then discusses that for a bequest for recitation to be valid, the recipient must be specified and the payment must be a gift (silah), not a wage (ujrah). The author criticizes the common practice of bequests to shaykhs for performing dhikr or khatm for the deceased, calling it hiring for obedience which is not a necessity. However, if the beneficiary is specified, it may be permissible based on the earlier reference from Muntakhab al-Zahiriyyah. The passage also includes a discussion on the wording 'al-niyabah tujzi' (whether with zay or jim) and a note from al-Hamawi questioning the term 'markab minhuma' (composite of the two).

Key Terms
نيابة (niyābah): Proxy: an appointed person acting on behalf of another to complete a religious duty.
عبادة مالية (ʻibādah māliyyah): Financial worship: acts involving expenditure of wealth, like zakat, sadaqah, expiations.
عبادة بدنية (ʻibādah badaniyyah): Bodily worship: acts requiring personal physical effort, like prayer, fasting, jihad.
مركب منهما (murakkab minhumā): Composite of both: worship involving both physical and financial elements, like Hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for proxy in Hajj to be valid?
A) The person is unable to perform Hajj for any reason, even temporary.
B) The person is permanently incapacitated until death.
C) The person is wealthy and cannot spare the time.
D) The person is a woman and cannot travel alone.

Key Takeaway
Proxy in worship is governed by the type of hardship: financial acts are fully delegable, bodily acts are non-delegable, and composite acts like Hajj are delegable only under the dispensation of permanent incapacity.

General Ruling on Deputyship in Worship: Classification and Proof
States the rule of niyaba in financial, bodily, and combined acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify acts of worship into financial, bodily, and combined, and state the Hanafi ruling on deputyship (niyaba) for each category, including the proof from istihsan.

Core Idea
Deputyship is valid in financial acts always, never in bodily acts, and only in combined acts when permanently unable.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi principle that divides acts of worship into three categories for the purpose of deputyship. The ruling is not arbitrary but based on the nature of hardship involved in each type of worship. We will explore the scriptural basis and the role of istihsan in the case of Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the matn, list the three categories: purely financial (maliyya), purely bodily (badaniyya), and combined (murakkab minhuma).
Examples: zakat, prayer, Hajj respectively.
2
State the ruling for each
Deputyship is valid in financial acts both when able and unable, never in bodily acts, and only in combined acts when permanently unable.
3
Understand the rationale
The purpose of obligations is personal hardship (al-ibtilā' wa al-mashaqqa). Bodily hardship cannot be transferred; financial hardship can.
In bodily acts, the deputy's action does not realize hardship on the principal.
4
Analyze the qiyas
Analogy (qiyas) would forbid deputyship in Hajj because it includes both hardships, and the bodily part is not satisfied by a deputy.
The text says: 'kāna muqtaḍā al-qiyās an lā tajrī al-niyāba fī al-ḥajj'.
5
Recognize the istihsan exception
The Lawgiver made a concession (rukhsa) for permanent inability, allowing the principal to bear the financial hardship and send a deputy.
Condition: permanent inability until death (al-ʿajz al-dāʾim).

I said: I saw in Sharh Tuhfat al-Muluk restricting it to supererogatory acts where he said: "It is valid for a person to assign the reward of his supererogatory worship to another, whether fasting, prayer, Quran recitation, charity, dhikr, or other types of good." But later at the end of the chapter, regarding the issue of a person who performs Hajj on behalf of his parents, he said it is valid i.e. assigning the reward to him, and we will mention there that Hajj is performed by the doer, and thus his obligation falls, and that is explicit regarding the intent. The text: 'النيابة تجزئ' with 'zay' and 'hamza' as in the handwriting of al-Iyasi and al-Ghazzi, and in some copies with 'jim' and 'ra' and 'ya' as in the handwriting of al-Razi and al-Ayni, and al-Zayla'i commented on it, and similarly afterwards: 'أجزاً' with hamza means 'he sufficed', and 'أجزى' without hamza means 'it sufficed'. Our shaykh from al-Shalabi. And it is said from 'جزأ' 'al-amr yajzi' as in 'قضى' in weight and meaning. As in the commentaries of Miskin. The text: "And regarding the combined type, al-Hamwi said in their statement 'combined from them' there is consideration because..."
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the general principle of deputyship (niyaba) in acts of worship according to the Hanafi school. The author divides worship into three categories: purely financial acts (like zakat, charity of fitr, expiations), purely bodily acts (like prayer, fasting, recitation), and acts combining both (like Hajj). The ruling is that deputyship is always valid in financial acts regardless of the principal's ability, never valid in bodily acts, and only valid in combined acts when the principal is permanently unable to perform them himself. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the purpose of bodily acts is personal hardship, which cannot be realized through a deputy; financial acts involve parting with wealth, which can be done by a deputy; combined acts like Hajj contain both elements, but analogy (qiyas) would forbid deputyship. However, the Lawgiver granted a concession (rukhsa) for the case of permanent inability, allowing the principal to send a deputy by bearing the financial hardship. This is a mercy, not a general rule. The passage also discusses related issues: the requirement to specify the agent (to avoid being a wage), the invalidity of hiring for recitation or tahleel, and the permissibility of transferring reward of supererogatory acts to others, with a difference of opinion regarding obligatory acts. The text also includes textual variants of the word 'تجزئ/تجزئ'.

Key Terms
النيابة (al-niyābah): Deputyship; acting on behalf of another in performing an act of worship
العبادات المالية (al-ʿibādāt al-māliyyah): Acts of worship that primarily involve wealth, e.g., zakat, charity
العبادات البدنية (al-ʿibādāt al-badaniyyah): Acts of worship that primarily involve bodily actions, e.g., prayer, fasting
المركب منهما (al-murakkab minhumā): Acts combining both body and wealth, e.g., Hajj
العجز الدائم (al-ʿajz al-dāʾim): Permanent inability, expected to last until death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, deputyship in Hajj is valid only when the principal is:
A) Able to perform it himself
B) Permanently unable until death
C) Temporarily ill
D) Old but able

Key Takeaway
Deputyship in Hajj is an exception to the rule that bodily acts cannot be delegated, allowed only for those permanently unable through the principle of istihsan.

advanced
10 min
Practical Conditions and Controversies: Reward Transfer and Hiring
Expands on conditions and controversies related to niyaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on transferring reward of obligatory vs supererogatory acts to others, and identify the condition that deputyship requires the deputy to be a donee (sila) rather than a wage earner (ujra).

Core Idea
Transferring reward of obligatory acts is disputed; deputyship requires the deputy to be a donee, not a hired worker.

In addition to the basic ruling, the Hanafi school addresses practical issues that arise when people attempt to deputize others for worship. This lesson covers two such issues: whether one can assign the reward of their obligatory worship to another, and the requirement that the deputy for Hajj (or similar) must be specified as receiving a gift, not a wage. We also examine the modern practice of hiring reciters for the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two issues
The passage discusses two distinct topics: (1) transferring the reward (thawab) of obligatory acts to others, and (2) the condition that the deputy must receive a gift (sila) not a wage (ujra).
These are extensions of the main deputyship ruling.
2
State the Hanafi view on reward transfer
The text mentions a difference: some scholars say transferring reward is valid for supererogatory acts but not for obligatory acts; others say it is valid for both but the obligation is not discharged.
The preferred view is that transferring reward of obligatory acts is not established (needs a transmitted text).
3
Understand the difference between obligatory and supererogatory
Obligatory acts are due from the individual; transferring reward does not lift the obligation. Supererogatory acts are free gifts, so reward can be assigned to others.
The text cites Sharh Tuhfat al-Muluk: it is valid to assign reward of supererogatory acts to others.
4
Identify the condition of gift vs wage
For deputyship in Hajj (and similar), the deputy must be specified as the recipient of a gift (sila), not a wage (ujra). Otherwise, the deputyship is invalid.
This is because hiring for obedience (ijāra ʿalā al-ṭāʿa) is generally not allowed.
5
Apply to modern practices
Bequeathing money to have someone recite tahleel or complete Quran recitations for the deceased is invalid if it is a wage for the act. Only if the legatee is specified and the payment is a gift may it be permissible.
This is based on the principle from Muntakhab al-Zahiriyya.

The Book of Hajj, Chapter on Hajj on Behalf of Another. The combined type [of act] is sufficient only in case of inability, and the condition is permanent inability until the time of death, and it is only for the one given to. And it is proper that it is not so. And the apparent meaning of their unrestricted statements necessitates that there is no difference between obligatory and supererogatory; it is valid: if one prays an obligatory prayer and assigns its reward to another, it is valid but the obligation does not fall from his liability because the absence of reward does not entail the absence of discharge from liability. I have not seen it transmitted. His statement: "Deputyship applies in financial acts of worship in case of inability and ability, and does not apply in bodily acts at all, and in the combined type it applies only in case of inability." It is an explanation of the division of worship into three categories: purely financial, like zakat, charity of fitr, manumission, feeding, clothing in expiations, tithe, and expenditures, whether it is purely worship or worship with the meaning of maintenance or maintenance with the meaning of worship, as is known in usul. And purely bodily, like prayer, fasting, i'tikaf, recitation of Quran, dhikr, and jihad. And combined from body and wealth, like Hajj. The principle is that the purpose of obligations is trial and hardship, and in body acts it is by fatiguing the soul and limbs with specific actions, and by the action of his deputy the hardship is not realized on himself, so deputyship is not allowed absolutely, neither in inability nor in ability. In financial acts, it is by reducing beloved wealth by delivering it to the poor, and that exists by the deputy's action. Analogy would dictate that deputyship should not apply in Hajj because it includes both bodily and financial hardships, and the former is not satisfied by the deputy; however, Allah Almighty granted a concession in its dropping by bearing the other hardship, namely spending wealth, in the case of continuous inability until death, as mercy and favor, by giving the expenses of Hajj to one who performs it on his behalf. Unlike the case of ability, he is not excused because his leaving it is nothing but preferring his own comfort over the command of his Lord, and thereby he deserves. This: it is necessary to designate the reader so that what is given to him is a gift and not a wage, otherwise it is invalid, as in the wills of Muntakhab al-Zahiriyya. This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the general principle of deputyship (niyaba) in acts of worship according to the Hanafi school. Key Terms
الصلة (al-ṣilah): A gift; what is given voluntarily without expectation of compensation
الأجرة (al-ujrah): Wage; compensation for a service
الإجارة على الطاعة (ijāra ʿalā al-ṭāʿa): Hiring for acts of obedience/worship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid and recommended
B) Makruh but valid

Key Takeaway
Valid deputyship requires the deputy to receive a gift (sila), not a wage (ujra); furthermore, transferring reward of obligatory acts is not established, while supererogatory acts may be gifted to others.
